RATING: 6
"A story with Lovecraftian elements, not a Lovecraftian story" is rather apt, as is a comparison to Indiana Jones in that it's less horror and more worldly romp. I'm not after a game that is specifically an Arkham game, so much as that thematic universe. To that end, this fits marvelously well. Yes, you throw dice constantly (and it's that unfortunate form of "decide first, random after"), but thats not the point. Eldritch Horror is an interesting exploration game with a decent narrative and story (which was my driving factor); one where an actress go to the gym to work out (increase strength rolls) and then buy a double barreled shotgun to eradicate monsters and close gates or we're headed toward victory and then my partner's dark pact comes due, and she turns and stabs me (a reformed cultist) and feeds me to the monsters while she romps off to (a shared) victory. Somewhat group dependent; I didn't particularly feel there was much down time, but I valued the story that was created (even if it wasn't necessarily similar to the stories, ymmv). We read encounters to each other, only reading the bits that were relevant to tell the story and getting into character when possible. Music, lighting, and people getting into it is what really drives EH. To that end, it needs the first expansion to break up the limited cycling of cards (which really hurts the narrative aspect). 

Overall, no regrets, even if it's not something I'd want to play constantly. In some ways, Mansions of Madness (2nd edition in particular) scratches a similar itch, but it's different. Comparatively, EH is less thematic in it's presentation and representation, includes less tension or intimacy with it's subject, but has the potential to be much more emergent than MoM2e does with it's limited (and driven) scenario count. Eldritch Horror has the serious potential to go the distance for tons of plays, where as MoM2e doesn't.

One of my strongest 6s/weakest 7s.

RATING: 8
If you have ever so much as pronounced "Cthulu", you need this game.  A slightly lighter weight version of Arkham horror, this game is still rich and complex and very Lovecraftian.

There's much to love about the game, starting with it's overall look and feel.  All of the parts are sturdy and of high quality construction, while also being beautifully designed and colorful.  This is one of the few games (perhaps along with Cataan) that we enjoy setting up almost as much as playing.

Once you're into game play, EH becomes very immersive.  First, there are all the characters complete with back-stories, special attributes and personalities.  Then there are all of the monsters against which intrepid investigators must fight.  Pulled from the pages of Lovecraft, there's something satisfying about challenging these Ancient spawn, even when you lose (which will be often: see below).  Finally, there's the necessity for co-operative play -- a mechanic of which my family is particularly fond.

Even with all of the positives ahead, do not undertake EH lightly.  Set up alone can take 30 minutes or more, particularly when players draw out character selection and team balancing.  Second, there are multiple steps and actions for each player per turn, often resulting in events that may, themselves, take multiple steps to resolve.  Then there are all of the missions players must fulfill before they get the chance to face the big boss.  If I can sum it all up in a word to describe the game, it's this: long.

And what's a long game worth if it's not also… hard!  How hard?  Well, players in this game often DIE, as in dead -- no longer able to function and lose all the stuff you just spent an hour accumulating -- dead.  Sometimes players get the pleasure of dying twice having taken up the fight in the form of another investigator.  

For some, the thought of playing a game for hours that they are very likely to lose just doesn't sound attractive.  I can understand.  If you're not rewarded by the game's atmosphere and immersion, then you're likely not to come back after your first epic beat-down.  If you stick with it, however, and just enjoy the ride, there will come a time when you are there to see an Ancient fall.  And that, my friends, is sweet, sweet nectar!

Overall, Eldritch Horror is a realistic, deeply engaging, sophisticated game that can deliver hours and hours of entertainment.  Find willing and committed gamer friends, get out some candelabras, creepy music, and plenty of snacks, and make a late night of Eldritch Horror as often as possible.

EH has a relatively steep learning curve, and set-up is longer than I prefer; however, once you get going it's an excellent experience. I love the co-op nature, the mechanics, the rush against time, the surprises, and the narrative theme. It's a long play, but each minute is great. After playing a few times, I still think what you get in the box is a good deal, but the card narratives get a bit repetitive. It sounds like the expansions are the cure, but that's what I'm craving next. Really great game all around.

RATING: 6
Much to like in the art and components, but the game forces you to ignore the exploration and narrative aspects and focus on frantically trying to close gates and fight random monsters. For me that means much of the interesting theme is lost in favour of a simplistic Pandemic-like puzzle, without the elegance of that game and instead just a lot of dice rolling. The game really punishes you if you don't follow the script. All the rushing around makes the world seem unthematically small, and the rate at which clues come and go is at odds with any idea that they are precious secrets. I think there's potentially a great game here but the Arkham Horror style gameplay somewhat spoils it for me.

RATING: 8.25
A game in which you can never do enough. Nothing is easy in Eldritch Horror. Just when you've put your thumb in one hole, preventing a leak, another one opens. What could easily become a frustration is tempered by the exciting pace and weird tales of Eldritch Horror. 

Frustrations give way to small victories and satisfied fist pumps when a monster is defeated or a gate is closed or a rumor is stamped out. Passing a test when your life is on the line is a burst of excitement that many games lack.

I will say that you have to be in the mood to play this game. Be ready for frustration, for bad rolls, for doom to progress when you least need it. And make sure your companions are ready for it. If there is anybody who would rather check the time on their phone than listen to an event unfold, you have a recipe for a long and bothersome game. The mood must be right. But when it is, you've got a fantastic time ahead of you.

RATING: 7.5
Having played and *wanted* to enjoy Arkham Horror, but unable to get past the overwhelming components and play time, I was anxious to give this supposed slimmed down version a try. I think it delivers very solidly. The system is quite trim now - where everything (I mean nearly *everything*) is placed on the tons of card decks. So it's a sprawling mess in that way - but it's better than ten zillion tokens of it's fat brother. To be sure, there's still dozens of tokens - but it all seems more manageable. The theme sure comes through with the load of text on every item in the game - characters and encounters. Love the gooey theme for sure - FFG is better behaved, but still seems to like tossing in game-ending cards as well. But if I'm longing for what amounts to an experience game of weird horror and have about 45-60 min per player to spend, this one does it better than most.

RATING: 6
Eldritch Horror was introduced to me as streamlined Arkham Horror which would've been great, since I like playing the latter one, but due to the enormous amount of time needed for the setup and moving the pieces around, I seldom get a chance. 

Well, Eldritch Horror wasn't exactly what I expected. Setup was still quite a chore and the game didn't move all that fast. Or should I say that the game ended fast in terms of completed rounds, but the absolute time it took was quite long. I found it quite disappointing to see that I only managed to move from Panama to New York and then to San Francisco during the 3,5 hours. Oh, I loved the story, and actually celebrated when my character died, but I wanted to do more than just to collect two clues, buy a train ticket, get ambushed by a monster, get stabbed into forehead and wake up in a unfamiliar ditch without a recollection of the events night before.

In a nutshell, the story was enjoyable but I didn't feel that I actually contributed to the outcome of the game in any significant way. The proportion of story telling and book keeping to actual playing is way off its mark here. I would play again but I would never consider buying the game knowing that it wouldn't actually get any table time. Should I have a suitable slot in my calendar, Arkham Horror would be my pick, since it has almost the same playing time but you get to do more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
I played this game just once, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

Despite being a popular game, I never liked Arkham Horror. I think it's too long and too shallow, as the game-play consists in rolling a lot of dice for a very long time. So when Eldritch Horror came along I wasn't interested, even when I heard that the design considerably improved upon its predecessor.

Yet, it's wrong to judge anything before trying it. So when I had the opportunity, I gave this game a try... and yes, it's better the Arkham Horror, but no, I still don't like it. 

Let's start with the positive aspects. First, I feel that the luck factor of the dice is mitigated way better in this game. Second, closing a portal doesn't basically mean that you skip a turn. Third, the story feels more cohesive. Fourth, I really really liked the new mechanic for Spells and Conditions where you don't know the consequences of your rolls. It's extremely thematic and makes for some very memorable moments.

At the same time, this design shows many of the same flaws of its predecessor. Eldritch Horror is again a game of rolling a lot of dice for a very long time... and I just can't get over this. Dice rolling can be a fun mechanism where it makes sense (i.e. short light games). Yet it's a sign of superficial and lazy game design, when a long complex game (like this one) relies this heavily on dice rolling. Dice rolling doesn't provide interesting decisions and that's what I would expect from a game that lasts so damn long!

Second issue... delayed. Come on! You were smart enough to realize that it wasn't fun to lose a turn in order to close a portal and then?! You put the same Monopoly mechanism back in the game design? Someone could say: "Oh, but most of the time you have the choice. You don't have to lose your turn if you don't want to." To that I say: "Yes, it's true... such a pity that the choice of not becoming delayed means usually not getting cool stuff and/or not getting to do cool stuff". I just don't understand how a modern game designer looks at its prototype and says: "You know what... let's make the player choose if they want to get bored now or later". Unbelievable.

Therefore I cannot enjoy this game. Once again FFG made a game with wonderful components and many good ideas, just to waste all of it due to some lazy game design and stubborn ameritrash mentality. This is so frustrating, because this could have really been a good game. At least Arkham had the excuse of being an older design. Eldritch Horror doesn't have any excuse. No thanks.

RATING: 9

RATING: 10
Delivers a strong narrative with massive replay capability. Plays in a couple of hours with fairly simple rules and a reasonable footprint (YMMV of course).

The 'reckoning' effects are excellent. What at first may appear to be a fairly minor condition or even an asset can turn sour, in some cases starting a chain of further conditions and reckonings that given time and enough bad luck eventually spirals down to catastrophe. 

The various encounter decks in the base game are quite limited and they're easy to burn through in a single session. However this is addressed by the Forsaken Lore expansion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Much more enjoyable than I was expecting: a really well thought out reworking of Arkham Horror.  Between them the two games do a decent job of covering five of my favourite 'Cthulhu' stories. AH invokes the last half of 'The Shadow over Innsmouth' and the back end of 'The Dunwich Horror': stories where some kind of New England eruption is underway.  EH invokes 'The Call of Cthulhu', 'At the Mountains of Madness', and maybe the back end of 'The Shadow Out of Time': investigation stories that piece stuff together from different places. Neither of them gets across the claustrophobic feel of 'The Whisperer in Darkness', 'The Haunter of the Dark', or 'The Case of Charles Dexter Ward'.  Both games have some out of place elements: shopping trips, Christian tropes, etc.  Those tend to pull me up more in AH, but EH isn't without them.

The link to those two great stories is certainly a reason to enjoy AH: I do prefer EH, however, for its rules, design, mechanics (double sided cards are a great rip from MoM), the strategy/ride balance, the global scope and the distinctive twist each AO brings to it. I had a lot of fun with AH, and now do the same with EH.

Update: raised the score because the expansions I've tried (the small ones) are working out well without bloat issues.

RATING: 7
A good game that is well thought out with a great Lovecraftian theme.
You and your team mates play as investigators/saviours that have to travel the world solving mysteries to halt the coming of the Ancient One, a godlike archdemon that will destroy the earth!

The game play works very well, player having 2 actions per turn (Move, rest, aquire assests, get train tickets etc...), then each player resolves an encounter at their location. The encounter you must perform is dependant upon what is at your location, if there is a monster you must fight it, if there is a token, you can choose to resolve it (Some are clues, some are rumours), or you can choose to resolve a random encounter card.
After actions and encounters are completed the mythos phase happens, where actions regarding demons, portals and doom counters are resolved.

I really enjoyed the game, the rules were quite easy to pick up, it was immersive and flowed very well, the only true gripe I have is the total lack of variety in encounter cards; I lost count of how many times we were each an actor in a play (You'll know the card I mean when you play the game). It seems strange to me that they've obviously taken the time to make a game that looks and plays so well then to really short change the players on encounters, especially when there is a whole phase of the turn dedicated to them. Maybe the expansions address this issue, but it's still quite cheeky to force people to buy expansions in order to have a game that feels completed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Another adventure game set in the world of Lovecraft. I liked the quality components and the cool artwork. The condition mechanic is a clever idea and works great. 
Gameplay is quite simple. You just move on the board and draw a card to see what you found. The card instructs you to pass a test. So you roll a dice to see what happens. 

Eldritch Horror is too simple for my taste. The game is supposed to be a cooperative one but I didn't see how you can actually help the other players. Also the card descriptions are too generic and didn't grab me. I expected that this game would tell me a story like Mansions of Madness where every card has a meaning and there is a story that makes sense.

RATING: 4
Everything wrong with Ameritrash in one box.

The double-sided spell cards *are* a great, *great* mechanical implementation of 'meddling with unknown forces', but the different Old Ones (at least in the base set) didn't feel sufficiently distinct from each other (in terms of the mechanical challenges they presented) for me to feel like the theme excused your otherwise bog-standard Ameritrash "do the obvious optimal move" 'adventure game' problems. 

A truly thematic game will give me in-game, mechanical motivations to *act* differently depending on which character I'm playing, but this game is so superficial, and there is so little room for strategy [not, mind you, because of the randomness, just because the game doesn't really ask you to make tense decisions or solve problems beyond incredibly dry and usually obvious short-term logistical ones] that there is no engine beneath the veneer of narrative supposed to be driving the thing. You can't really make multiple valid decisions about how to approach the game's problems based on what YOU want to do and the expressiveness thematic games promise is thus really nowhere to be found. This would be fine if it didn't take 3 hours+ to play it, but the time:interesting problems ratio in this one is appalling.

RATING: 9
It's a globe spanning adventure with horrific mysteries to solve and sanity-shattering forces to combat. But ...
It just cannot hold a candle to Arkham Horror for us. Despite it being wider in scope it doesn't feel as immersive. The concept of solving mysteries to accomplish your goals instead of closing gates is novel but in order to make that goal viable the game must have a timer. And the mythos deck serves that purpose. But we just do not like that timer aspect. Plus if the mystery cards get drawn in a certain order you can be stalled out quite quickly. During one game fighting Cthulhu, the epic monster corresponding to a certain mystery card came out and we were never able to defeat it. 
So while Eldritch Horror draws you into a more expansive world the gameplay, unfortunately, contracts the experience in such a way that it feels smaller somehow. 
It does however play in less time and gives you a more themtic game so it is a double-edged sword. It's best to think of Eldritch as a globe-spanning adventure and Arkham as a claustrophobic run through the haunted town.

RATING: 9
Para mí una versión mejorada del Arkham horror. Corrige muchos de sus problemas pero empeora en un par de cosillas: 

El movimiento necesitaría revisarse. Teniendo en cuenta el tamaño del mapa y la necesidad de viajar por todo el mundo para resolver los misterios, encontrar pistas etc, es casi contradictorio que solo te dejen moverte de una zona a la adyacente. (O dos zonas si gastas todo tu turno en preparar el viaje y mover)

La otra pega que le veo es que le falten más cartas de encuentros, pistas, etc... No es que sea algo fundamental para disfrutar del juego pero siempre es más interesante encontrarte con sucesos desconocidos que repetir una y otra vez los mismos de siempre. La ampliación de Yig mejora muchísimo este aspecto.

Aun así me encanta, y es posiblemente uno de mis juegos favoritos. (Por lo que a mí respecta es muy probable que este juego salga más veces a la mesa que el propio Arkham Horror)

RATING: 8.5
Desde mi punto de vista este juego es una evolución muy mejorada de Arkham Horror. 

Como parte positiva:

- Se trata de un juego muy narrativo, con ciertas pinceladas de rol, en el que pasan muchas cosas muy variopintas. Eso le da mucha variedad aunque la mecánica principal sea bastante sencilla y accesible a cualquier tipo de jugador.

- Corrige y evoluciona las mecánicas farragosas de AH y las resuelve en la mayor parte de los casos de forma sencilla y elegante. Como consecuencia el juego transcurre de forma mucho más fluída y ágil (aunque igual que AH no es nada corto).

- Gran calidad de producción. Buenos materiales y componentes en general.

- Si te gusta H.P.Lovecraft es el juego que en mi opinión mejor sintetiza todos los elementos de su obra (algo harto difícil). AH lo intenta también pero es mucho menos redondo.

Como parte negativa:

- Al final, si tienes poca capacidad de inmersión o no te gusta H.P.Lovecraft, el juego consiste en cerrar portales, matar monstruos y resolver las cartas de misterio con acciones más o menos rutinarias (hay muchos más detalles que a mi me apasionan, pero simplificando se trata de eso). Hay gente a la que esto le va a parecer repetitivo.

- Más que un juego es una experiencia... y muy influída por la suerte. Las tiradas son importantes y el factor suerte está muy presente. Si no salen los éxitos en los dados las cosas se torcerán. Si te gusta el control y la planificación a largo plazo... este no es tu juego.

- Algo escaso de cartas de algunos tipos. Eso se solventará con las expansiones (maldito EDGE).

- Larga duración (para mí no es un problema).

   A mi el juego me ha gustado bastante y me ha parecido divertido. Me ha sorprendido mucho su agilidad y fluidez dentro de los muchos detalles que trata de tener en cuenta.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
A good revision of Arkham Horror with most of the mechanics well refined, specially the investigator skills and actions, and the function of monsters, portals, clues and the items in the game. 

Length and density that Arkham had have been improved too. Eldritch is shorter and more flexible. 

The narrative of the game is well done now. The aim of the game is not to close portals anymore but to solve some narrative mysteries that depend on the old one.

The items and conditions are awesome, with some side effects that the players dont know till the right moment. Dark Pact is simply great. 

In spite of all these improvements I still love Arkham more. Eldritch needs more expansions and material. When it get them will be better than Arkham without doubt. 

-----

Una buena revisión de Arkham con casi todas sus mecánicas depuradas, especialmente las habilidades y acciones de los investigadores y el papel de los monstruos, portales, pistas y objetos en el juego.

La longitud y densidad de Arkham también han sido mejoradas. Eldritch es más corto y flexible.

La narrativa del juego está bien conseguida ahora. Ya no consiste en cerrar portales, sino en resolver cartas de misterio que dependen del primigenio.

Las cartas de objeto y condición son maravillosas, con algún efecto secundario que no se activa hasta el momento adecuado y que los jugadores desconocen. Pacto Oscuro es simplemente genial.

A pesar de todas sus mejoras, me sigue gustando Arkham más. Eldritch necesita más expansiones y material, una vez las tenga será sin duda la mejora definitiva de Arkham.

RATING: 10
I have played this game a ton and is one of my favorite games of all time.  The 10 rating is for the game with all expansions included.  On it's own, the base game is probably about an 8 with many of the expansions ranging from 7 to 8 themselves.  However, when you get a big game with everything included, this game is amazing!  

It has a nice amount of roleplay and story elements along with a bit of character advancement.  The game is decently challenging, making each game intense and enjoyable.  I have played this game so many times, I have lost count (probably around 20 to 30 times).  I have started a game with friends to finish around 10:30 at night and we each look at one another and decide to play again, going until 2:30 in the morning.

I feel this is one of the best games to capture the Lovecraftian Universe.  Games take around 2 to 4 hours to play, depending on the scenario along with about 30 minutes of setup time (I can usually do it in 15 mins due to the organization system I have set up).  I have gone out and purchased card holders for this game to make management of card decks and set up times efficient.  I am always willing to play this and don't think that will ever change.

RATING: 10
Surprisingly easy to learn.

The story comes across mostly by cards (encounter phase). Usually you read some flavour text on the card and then resolve some test (roll dice), sometimes followed by another diferent test, either resulting in sucess or fail. But if you don't like the reading you can simply look at what icon it shows and roll test for that particular icon. If you don't like rolling dice then this game is not for you, because you will be rolling dice a lot.

On your turn, you can move, collect train or boat tickets (to use while moving), rest, trade, buy assets, and do actions printed on cards and character sheet. Then everyone has some kind of encounter (monster, location, gate, clue, expedition, rumor, mystery cards) and then one mythos card is drawned and resolved.

Really the drive here is the story. You have a lot of generic story but also, when resolving clues, you have story that is related to the Ancient One you are facing, in that particular game.

The main objective of the game is solving 3 diferent misteries regarding the Ancient One (The game provides 4 Ancient Ones to choose from). As gates keep opening up between worlds, and with them lots of monsters keep coming, you have to find a balance between closing down gates and still persuing the Ancient One misteries.

Lots of diferent Characters to choose from, with diferent stats, and lots of spells and diferent kind of weapons. A bit too much for non-gamers.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I've played this 4 times so far, but every time there's been at least 1 newbie, so take this with a grain of salt.

There's a lot of rules to keep track of. But, the game flows easily enough as long as at least one or two people playing know what to do. It'll just require that person to "run" the game for a while before everyone gets caught up to speed. 

In every game, the newbs learned what to do after a few rounds, so it required less micromanaging by myself/other "experienced" players.

I've played Azathoth twice (once with 2 people, once with 3), Yog Sothoth once (3 people), and Hastur from one of the expansion packs (5 engineering students that thought "how hard could it be?" and were promptly destroyed). Each one seemed balanced. Even when we got mauled by Hastur, it felt like the result of bad strategy/unlucky dice rolls instead of an overpowered enemy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.9
While Elder Sign sucked me in, I wanted to have more ...

So I decided to take the plunge and buy Eldritch Horror. And then it dawned on me ... this is the kind of game I love (and have been searching for all my life)...

Lots of flavor text, lots of Lore.

"Pure" gamers who want to win in Eurogames will probably find it not competitive enough or too long or too random. But I don't play games "to win". Theme these days is the number one in great games. If you look at mechanics only, keep playing the aging Eurogames. 

But ...

I play games to have adventures and experience the fun and story telling of a correct setting.

Eldritch Horror offers this in spades. 

Good advice: take your time to watch the many Youtube videos how to learn to play and then take the dive.

I think it has only a 4/10 complexity if you take your time to learn the flow of the game.

Top materiel for SOLO play with 2 Investigators.

Best rulebook of Fantasy Flight Games in recent years.

EDIT: Notched it up to 9.8 after an epic loss. Seriously, this is no longer a game but an extensive walk in a story of HP Lovecraft.

EDIT 2: 9.9. While Mansions of Madness is equally good, the solo experience puts this 0.1 point ahead as my number one game second only behind Labyrinth war on Terror, while MoM is really out of category.

What an experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A streamlined reworking of Arkham Horror, but not a replacement for it. Thematically it's more globe-trotting adventuring than pure horror, and also not as tense. Mechanically it's cleaner, but still a lot for newcomers to take in. It's shorter, but play time still balloons with 4+ players. I like it and I think the Indiana Jones flavor can be ramped up with expansions, but I'd still take the flavor of Arkham over this in most instances. 

Edit: I'm happy to say this game is becoming more flavorful with more expansions added in, especially the larger ones.

The more I play this game more I'm convinced it's really not much less fiddly than Arkham, and those mechanics not as immersive as the fiddly stuff in Arkham. However, unlike Arkham this game feels stronger with more expansions added in.

RATING: 5
You HAVE to be in the right mood.  This is pure American-themed gameplay: awesome artwork, lots of fiddly bits, lots of combat (sort of), and LOTS of dice rolling.  While there is certainly strategy involved, the game still takes you on a ride.  It does things TO you rather than you controlling IT.  And the odds are pretty good that the game will really smack you around.

So get into character, invest in the story, and enjoy the ride.  If you do, you'll likely have a good time.  If you don't, it'll be a dice rolling exercise in futility.  And at 45 minutes per player (30 is really pushing it if you engage with the story and read the narrative bits), that can feel very, very long.

UPDATE: Eventually, I soured on the experience and sold the game.  I'm a strategy gamer at heart and also not big on co-ops (Pandemic Legacy being the exception).  This was a swing and a miss but I'm glad I tried it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Pro's:

1) The art and the story the game tells, the mood and the setting itself.
2) Variety of cards and locations and ridiculous events that can happen. Example, taking a debt to purchase a shotgun only to use the same shotgun on the guys that come to collect the debt.
3) The build up of the tension as the world starts to completely fall apart and you are in a desperate battle against the minions of the elder gods or the elder gods themselves.

Con's: 

1) The length of the game coupled with the reliance on dice rolls can sometimes feel really mean. Even if you work your hardest to mitigate, it can be really frustrating to then lose.
2) The game needs an expansion to get the needed variety, furthermore there are a lot of pieces and cards and bits that can sometimes make the game feel super fiddly.
3) Totally minor issue, but why on earth did they not ship the game with more dice.
4) I feel like a lot of the game is basically just mathing out which characters should be played at what player count. For example always picking Charlie Kane at 4 players. Why is the best way to win to basically play it incorrectly.

Addendum: This game has gotten worse and worse with subsequent plays. It's hard to say exactly what it is, but ultimately It never feels like I'm actually experiencing a world of eldritch horror where my sanity slowly erodes. The way the player actually interact mechanically and what the game is spelling out for me in terms of theme never really gels well for me. It feeds the theme to you, but the problem is this works one way only. Normally when a game feels thematic it acts as positive feedback loop. This on the other hand doesn't, basically it is a puzzle. I am hoping that Arkham Horror the card game does what this game could not.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I previously rated this game real low...But turns out, my game group had somehow made a ton of mistakes in learning the rules to this game, leading to a very lax might-as-well-do-everything-because-why-not type game that never seemed to end.

Oh god was that so wrong.

Sure it can start deceptively obvious with what you should do, but then the board starts getting real crowded with all these portals, monsters, clues, and rumors all over the world while the ol' doom clock keeps ticking faster and faster. This easily gives me the senses of impending doom and urgency that I crave to have in my cooperative games. 

And yet it still giving you the ability to find crazy artifacts that allows you to slay everything in your path! But at the same time you could still lose it all in the toss of a die roll...Which can be avoided by resting so you're more likely to survive the next encounter! But do you really want to take turns to rest when more and more terrible things keep popping up all around the world, that you probably should be addressing!? It's these hard decisions that I love so much! 

Granted, I'll never ever ever play this with those who are prone to over analyzing every possible move for every other person at the table ever again...But, why in the world would you want to play any co-ops with those types anyway? 

Now that I own this, I will probably waste no time in grabbing up the many expansions. I'm excited. But oh, will my wallet weep.

RATING: 9
I never play Arkham horror.  But Eldritch horror is a EPIC game were you are few investigaters trying to protect the world from the ancien gods (in vain for most of my played XD).  
Pros:  Good immersion in the Lovecraft world.  I really feel like each of the ancien gods are a big threat against the world.  A must for Lovecraft fans. The game is hard... but i mean really hard (i only win once on 7 plays), but you will have fun trying to beat the game!  When I won the game (against the easiest ancien Azatoth) I was so happy, it was rewarding (and it was a close win).  The encounter are fun and it had some history for your investigators.  The co-op in the game is great, we need to help each other to get the full potential of your team, and you sure need it. 
Cons: it is a long game (4 hour minimum, and the more player you play, the more long it will be), and that why i don't play as i would like. It says 1-8 players on the box... i will never play this game more than 4 players, never...  There is little rules hard to remember, but the reference book is really helpfull.  

I promise my friend to pay them their drink next time, if we win, it get more intense and fun ;P.  One of my favorite game, im going to get at least one small expension for this one for more of the good stuff.

RATING: 9
I held off on this game. As Undead Viking has said, it felt disloyal to Arkham to embrace it. I played once (had to, of course) and I didn't like it. I had to reshuffle the Mystery cards back into the deck and the operation felt like Chutes and Ladders. Was it streamlined, yes, clearly...but that initially made me like it LESS. I liked the swagger of being able to handle the setup, fiddly rules and simple game management of Arkham. That feels like a side game, like part of the ritual.

I kept thinking about EH, though, how I could have prepared myself better for those Mystery draws. I thought about different strategies. I played again and liked it a good deal more. I knew what I was doing, even if I were losing. It felt more like a story. I also saw how the Reckoning and Spell mechanics not only were much more elegant mechanics that what Arkham offers, but they also supported the narrative more. The different GOO's providing different encounters is a huge improvement. I still haven't won, but I am intrigued by this game. I may have to admit that it is replacing Arkham for me. It still doesn't feel quite like it has the richness of Arkham, but the game at this point only has one expansion. I am looking forward to the ride.

Update: I've had a flurry of playing EH and, pound for pound, it's now better than Arkham for me. I've won sessions, I've lost sessions; all the sessions have been really fun and thematic. It's a solid 9 - and I feel perhaps only one or two expansion mechanics away (for added richness) from being a 10.

RATING: 4
Take a great RPG-esque adventure game (Arkham Horror) but streamline some mechanics and sterilize the whole thing and you've got Eldritch Horror.

When they streamlined/sterilized AH, they got rid of everything that made Arkham Horror great.
This is no longer an adventure game, it's a puzzle game along the lines of Pandemic. Unfortunately, unlike Pandemic, it's still got a ton of fiddly bits and many decks of cards just like it's forefather Arkham Horror.

So, if you're looking for an adventure game that will leave you with cool stories to tell afterwards, look elsewhere.
If you're looking for a game like Pandemic but longer and with a load of bits and cards, you've found your game.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I love this game. It's thematic, tense, and plays pretty smoothly. It can seem quite random at times (and in some sense it certainly is) but as you get experienced you'll find there are many things you can do to tip the odds in your favor. It helps that I really like cooperative games generally. I would much rather work with my friends than try to crush them (or, more likely, be crushed by them.)

The replayability is excellent with a 12 different investigators and 4 different Ancient Ones in the box. The number of possible combinations is huge. Furthermore, because of the luck of the draw, even games with the same investigators against the same opponent will play out vastly differently.

The investigators are not quite balanced (Charlie Kane and Jacqueline Fine are head and shoulders above the rest), but this is not really an issue in a co-op game. They are all nicely thematic and most have interesting abilities. Two favorites of mine are Lily Chen, the martial artist who can train her skills faster than other investigators, and Diana Stanley, the ex-cultist with powerful magic but a fragile psyche.

The Ancient Ones present four different themes. Cthulhu (the hardest) deals in sanity damage and sea encounters. Shub-Niggurath floods the board with monsters. Yog-Sothoth (the easiest) interacts with spells. Finally, Azathoth's shtick is that he (it?) will immediately devour the world if Doom is allowed to run out. They all provide significantly different games.

The game is also highly flexible with regard to player count. It plays quite well at anything from 1 to 6 players. (It can even support 7 or 8, but I think the game starts to bog down at that point.) The optimal number of players is probably 3 or 4.

A nice aspect of the game is that the difficulty can be easily adjusted in a number of ways. The win rate for four players using the default rules against an average Ancient One is roughly 60% (though player skill will make a significant difference here); with some simple adjustments, you can probably get your expected win rate anywhere from 20% to 90%. So whether you prefer wins to be rare events or the usual outcome, Eldritch Horror can accommodate that. Personally, I find the default settings a little too generous to the players. I recommend removing the easy Mythos cards and perhaps spawning an extra Gate at setup.

One issue with the base game is a relative lack of cards. Most encounter decks only have 8 cards in them, which is not enough. You'll become overly familiar with the encounters quite quickly. I therefore recommend picking up the Forsaken Lore expansion, which will solve this issue.

The biggest downsides of this game are probably the setup and storage issues. Setup is typically a ~20 minute process. The game box does not provide any decent way of storing the game's innumerable cards and tokens, so you'll have to come up with something yourself.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Blech. I'll start with some small positives. There is a good theme and some sense of progression with leveling up your characters and obtaining items.

This, Talisman, and others in the same family are simply not my kind of game. 

All of that said, although I don't absolutely love Arkham Horror, for some reason it sticks out as being measurably more enjoyable than Eldritch in my mind. This is one of my worst game experiences in recent memory, especially for a game as well regarded as this one is.

RATING: 9
Lot of controversy about this game vs Arkham Horror.  I have owned and played both and this is my short and sweet comparison.  Keep in mind this takes into account my specific gaming group.

Arkham Horror vs Eldrich Horror
1.  Eldrich duration is better due to its quicker overall playing time.
2.  Eldrich plays quicker per player as the turns are broken up in a way that leaves players active for shorter periods but more frequent.
3.  Eldrich less prone to affect my AP players.
4.  Arkham has tons more variety.
5.  Eldrich builds a more cohesive story
6.  Arkham has lots of cool pieces.
7.  Eldrich setup is easier.
8.  Arkham has a more creepy dark alley overtone which does a better job of elevated the horror aspect.  Eldrich seems more adventurish.

So, basically, when we play, we prefer Eldrich.  If we played it more frequently, we would probably prefer Arkham due to the more variation.  Arkham typically made it to the table once a year.  Eldrich will likely average three to four.  

2017 Update:  Lots of expansions have given Eldritch the variation it needed.  Still going strong.

If you like pandemic and Lovecraftian Lore this game is a great pick up for you. If you're on the fence with pandemic I don't feel like this offers enough of a change to be worth it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is definitely cleaner than Arkham Horror, but maybe not clean enough yet for my taste.

The flavor is still good, but doesn't work as well for me here as it did in Arkham. I think I just prefer the art direction and more intimate setting of Arkham. Globetrotting feels pretty weird for Lovecraft.

As a narrative experience, this is much like Arkham. There are some neat things that can happen through play (particularly with the new Condition/Spell cards and their secrets on the back) but reading all the flavor text from the various mythos cards, etc., tells an incoherent story at best.

The Conditions deck is probably my favorite part of the game. It's a massive simplification of the separate injury/madness/blessing/curse decks from Arkham, and the fact that you don't know quite what you're getting into with each one you take leads to a lot of excitement.

I also really like that each Old One now has set objectives and mystery cards rather than just having to close X gates. The mystery cards improve the storytelling.

All in all, if I'm in the right mood this is pretty engaging. I guess I just don't see the need for a game this random to take this long.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
update Aug 2015

The last year hasn't been kind to Eldritch.  As my comments below indicate, we didn't hate the game but we didn't love it either.  But let's face it, "mediocre" doesn't earn a place in the rotation let alone one on the shelf and as time has past Eldritch finds itself relegated to the shelf.

While Eldritch gathers dust, Arkham Horror has been played at least twice.  Why?  

Arkham presents the Cthulhu mythos with more flavor, the gamers become immersed in the story as they play.  Arkham develops real tension as well, it pulls the gamers into the swirling madness.  More importantly, while remaining quite a luck fest, Arkham gives gamers more real choices.  It's simply a better _game_, something you play whereas Eldritch often feels more like something that plays while you watch.  

Eldritch fails to come off the shlef for several reasons.  First, its replayability is poor right out of the box.  We all know Fantasy Flight is going to make expansions, but Eldritch suffers from the _need_ to be expanded.  A need that's apparent the first game you play.  Thinking that we'd throw good money after bad just to make a base game replayable is a bit patronizing.  Hook us with a good game, and we'll buy more.  

Secondly, Eldritch isn't very good at what it does.  As a globe trekking coop, it falls flat when compared to other members of the field.  It simply doesn't offer enough real choices to players while pouring dice over them.  

As a Cthulhu'ian storytelling engine it pales when compared to every other Cthulhu themed game I can think of except that silly Yahtzee one.

As a three to four hour long game.  No, there's just no way we'd choose Eldritch over virtually any other game(s) to play for four hours.  And let's not sugar coat it, three to four hours is what we're talking about here.

Bottom line, Eldritch is a game we wanted to love, it sounds good in theory, but it just doesn't do anything well enough to justify table time and if it can't make it to the table, why have it in our collection?

-------------------------------------------------

6+ plays both solo and groups of 3 or 4.

I enjoy this game quite a bit.  It's true, the cards are thin, the board potato chips, and there are too few encounter cards, but it works well as a game.  

The problem with the encounter cards stems from basic arithmetic, half the sites on the map are cities so "it always seems" you're pulling encounter cards to read their city text.  In fact, the majority of the time you are on cities at turn end.  Given each board region has only eight encounter cards and the GOO's clue/research deck has twelve, we found encounters, particularly research encounters repeating even during our first play (I swear to all old ones if I see that damn "metallic crate with legs" one more time!).  

With time I found two aspects of the game that bother me more.  First, Blessings are rather hard to achieve in EH.  Gone is the Church and one's ability to chose to buy a blessing.  Now there's a spell that grants a blessing, but that's problematic and requires both an action be spent and that two investigators are in the same location.  Random encounters grant blessings as well, but we've found we miss the ability to "go get blessed".  Worse perhaps, the ability to remove a curse is more difficult as well.  Regardless, by making blessings rare, difficult and random, the designers have removed an important tactical tool; removed one of the last ways that players can control their fate.  And in Eldritch, you simply don't have the time to waste running around cursed. 

Which brings me to my biggest problem with the game.  Eldritch introduces a predetermined turn clock, the mythos deck's card count.  Investigators have X turns in which to accumulate and spend the clues, spells, and actions necessary to solve the GOO's three (or four) mysteries.  If the deck runs out ... the game ends.  No Big Bang, no GOO awakening, no thematic icing on the cake of the past three hour session.  The game simple ends.

In over half a dozen plays we have either won (rarely) or lost by mythos deck exhaustion.  Only one time did doom reach 0 and the GOO awaken, and even then a turn later the mythos deck ran out.  And there's something deeply dissatisfying about losing because the deck runs dry.  We immersed ourselves in a story for a few hours only to have the illusion shatter and the game be revealed in its mechanical barrenness.

Are we playing sub-optimally?  Of course, but we're playing the way we enjoy.  We're certainly good at closing gates, which drive the doom track.

The game's strength stems from its focus on solving GOO specific clue/research encounters and GOO mystery "tasks".  Gone are the clues you simply "pick up", here in Eldritch you might spend several turns in a location trying to earn the clue token located there.  This is thematically fantastic, but burns the limited encounter deck even quicker.  Imagine, it takes you three turns to earn a clue token, there went a quarter of the GOO's twelve research encounter cards for that location type.  Given half the clues will be in cities, and that the most profitable locations in which "to camp" while repeatedly trying to solve clues are cities ... you can see that during a game the research decks cycles three or more times, with most city encounters appearing twice.  Park an investigator (like Mr Kane) in London to generate clues, many of which appear where the investigators desire, often a city, and the effect gets even worse.

Those three turns also cost you almost a quarter of the mythos clock deck, and I'm not sure what you can do about it.

Listen, the math is simple: each investigator gets two actions a turn, and the mythos deck has 16 cards, that's 32 actions your investigator gets a game, assuming they're not detained.

Where the game succeeds is in delivering a reasonably cohesive narrative set in the Cthulhu mythos.  Something that Arkham can sometimes completely fail to do.  It's true, some encounters just seem random, but they seem random in the normal "shit happens" sort of way we all experience in life.  Given that the research encounters and GOO mysteries are tied to the GOO, a strong skeleton of a narrative exists if nothing else.

I'd also mention that one does identify with their investigators during play.  Investigators are quite quirky and yet seem balanced.  You find yourself loving the investigator who always passed critical tests with just a die or two.  Investigators can also improve their skill attributes, allowing players to customize their character to some degree.  True, it may be sub-optimal play to waste precious mythos cards (i.e. time) improving your character, but it makes the game more engaging.  Besides if you find yourself with a choice between moving over two turns to a trouble spot via a space that might grant you an improvement or moving through one that is likely not to ... you take the path of improvement.  As these locations are all cities in regions, once again you can see the burden on the city encounter portion of the eight card region decks. 

Investigators can also acquire Conditions, further quirks that often aren't very severe, but give you the choice to burn actions resting in the hopes of alleviating the condition, or risk its wrath if you roll badly during reckonings.

While I've found the use of a rulebook and separate reference guide works surprisingly well, invariably several critical rules or nuances are "buried" in the reference guide.  Here are a few critical examples to watch for in play.  

(1) Clues are discarded, pitched out of play until the draw cup runs empty, and not recycled to the draw cup so that in a given game clues will not appear twice in the same place unless the entire cup empties and is refilled.  To a degree this helps to keep the research encounter deck from wearing thin as quickly.

(2) Investigators can only use one bonus from an item or ally ... we skip the ally clause of this and limit investigators to a single item in combat but all the allies they can use (which usually isn't many).

(3) Combat is handled differently then in Arkham although the symbols and graphical presentation is virtually identical.  Re-read the combat section and the accompanying example.  We played a couple games (and enjoyed the game) playing combat in the incorrect Arkham style which basically results in less sanity loss and more stamina loss.  Bottom line, neither test is "all or nothing" as in Arkham.

(4) The game's difficulty can be scaled by removing particularly difficult cards (with tentacles) or 'easy' ones (blue ice).  There's no shame in this as the game is still difficult for newcomers playing in the "easy" configuration while the "normal" difficulty level can be positively nightmarish with bad card draws.

We choose to ignore cards that make you throw away completed mysteries.  That's simply a rubbish mechanic.  Nikki, how could you let Corey get that one by? 

As for comparisons to Arkham.  They both have a place in my heart.  I would stress that Eldritch isn't significantly shorter to play, and not much easier to teach, but it does have less rules "to remember" during play.  The machine has less parts to keep track of, and those parts it does have are easier to track through better card ergonomics and token use.  For example, monsters rarely if ever move, so right there, you've eliminated "types" of monsters, monster movement arrows, the sky, etc.  The few monsters that do move either move to a home location when they enter the board or they move during the Reckoning resolution (with both types clearly indicated by an icon on their face and text on their back).

Eldritch Horror is far from perfect.  In revisiting the Arkham approach to Cthulhu, Eldritch delivers a more cohesive narrative and requires less mental overhead to track details of the game engine.  This latter aspect may let some players (such as myself during solo play) focus more on the story and strategy of the game while becoming more immersed in the narrative and their investigator's fate.  But the narrative is still fueled by dice and some may find the game too chaotic or worse, repetitive.  Sadly, the limited encounter card pool and the artificial mythos clock work strongly against the narrative.  In fact, it's safe to say I found the game's narrative stronger during our first few plays.  New GOO refresh things a fair amount as the mysteries and research encounters change, but I worry that the game is becoming a mechanical exercise of skill tests

Bottom line, while I like a lot about Eldritch Horror, I suspect that it will not have the longevity that Arkham has showed even with the inevitable expansions.  That having been said, we have enjoyed the game for at least 6 plays, so you get a good bang for your buck.  I'd recommend fans of the mythos as well as folks who like challenging co-op games give Eldritch serious consideration.


As a variant, I think that the next time we play we'll add a fourth level of mythos cards of the same mix as present in the third level in hopes of lengthening the game clock to the point where the game ends for some thematic reason and not just because the rules say so.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
I find myself hesitating before I label a thematic FFG game as elegant but that is how I feel.  The conditions cards are wonderful way to handle a multitude of subsystems that made Arkham Horror increasingly fiddly. If FFG can stick to this format as much as possible with its expansions the rules overhead from added variety will be be trivial.

The reckoning events and associated iconography again seems a simple mechanical method of keepng track of triggered events.  The interaction between  the triggered events the old ones, monsters, conditions, and items add at lot of depth without complicated tracking.  Just look for the reckoning event icons and follow the instructions on the cards.

All of the encounter cards are resolved in a similar manner (even the "complex ecounters" are not that different).  Despite this streamlined process variety has not been lost.

The player still has to keep track of lots of item and character specific powers (actions and modifiers for instance) can get a bit overwhelming in a long game.  The rules around the old ones, tracking doom and the end game are somewhat less streamlined and lack the previously mentioned elegance.

I do not miss the character sliders, the maintenance phase, money, stackable dice modifiers, being trapped in a location and most importantly the reduced player agency that I associate with Arkham Horror. 

New material has integrated well, and plays well solo.

What I do think on the other hand it is I'm looking for more things in a ~2,5-3 hours long game. There was almost no strategy, no real cooperation you just do your stuff, roll the dice, be happy or swallow the bitter pill and move on. If it would be 60 mins or less I'd buy it.

Update from 5 to 4:
I refuse to play it.

RATING: N/A
Typical FFG product, typos on game pieces, rule book, they love to phone it in. Elder Sign was another class act, typos that changed game play, very poor rule book, 6 month wait for an FAQ, rule lawyers paradise. FGG is one publisher I usually avoid, they just don't improve, Mayfair Games the other one, I don't like their market manipulation, nor their convoluted reasoning to do so. My LGS are nothing more then comic or sport card shops, tokenly into board games, milking the collectible cards for all they are worth.

Rule of thumb stay away from FFG's first prints.

Eldritch Horror isn't a bad game, but as is more of a demo game. The problem is Forsaken Lore is needed to fill out the cards. Forsaken Lore should have been in EH, instead FFG's milks us again. Forsaken Lore is not an expansion.

They also seem to want to bloat EH like AH. That Mountain of Madness is the start, next Under the Pyramid, this side board crap is to be expected. The Focus mechanics found in MoM should have been in Eldritch Horror. It should be included in reprints of EH and added to EH's base game rules. Moot point, you can easily added it, you just have to know about it. The other thing that sucks is the tiny cards as is par for FFG, shrinking components. These card size borders on a deal breaker, I don't enjoy struggling with cards like this. They are a pain.

The two small box expansions are worth getting if picked up cheap, not worth the faux MSRP, jury still out on the 2 side board expansions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6
Although it is for up to 8 players I would never play with more than 4 and I enjoy it best with 2.  Otherwise it becomes a very long winded game.  

Cons:

- Eventually you run out of fresh cards and start to hear the same flavor text over and over, at which point it begins to feel like a grind. The expansions help remedy this.

- This game suffers a rounding error where it plays better with even numbers of characters than with odd numbers. 

- Winning is not an exciting event.  At some point you meet the conditions and then you are finished.  You feel more satisfaction and relief than triumph. 

Pros:

- characters being able to die and other characters being able to pick up their dropped items. I highly suggest not getting too attached to a character.  You can easily churn through two or three in one game.

- obtaining items from the reserve is straightforward

- a good selection of characters and backgrounds

Overall, it feels much like a simplified Arkham Horror, but with fewer scenarios to play out.  As more expansions are released, more scenarios become available.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Too long and slow. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Finally got a chance to play with my brother and his wife. She doesn't gravitate towards these kinds of games. There is too much stuff going on and they don't grab her attention. But she really loved this game. I must say that our play through was just awesome! It started off slow since we were all pretty much learning how to play at the same time. By the end of our game, we were discussing meaningful strategy. Had we had one more round, we would have won. We just couldn't get to the target location on time. We were all one space short. We were hoping to get lucky on the mythos draw. But no dice. 

Man it was intense! I can't say enough how fun and exciting this game was. Looking forward to another run...

Update: The more I play Eldritch Horror and become more aware of the tactical decisions in regards to mitigating your risks and the more I realize the value of certain strategies at certain times, the more I absolutely love this game. You mitigate risk. Yes, there are random elements. But you can greatly mitigate your risks. And the extra random elements fit nicely with the theme. This is a suspense/horror game after all. Having elements within the game that lie outside your control is fantastic and fits with the theme. Only an idiot would not get that. I've seen people rag on both Arkham Horror and Eldritch Horror for that aspect. I've never played Arkham Horror, but it has always appealed to me. I've been tempted to buy it even after having Eldritch Horror. While having similar elements, they are vastly different games. I just haven't done so because of the large footprint, and having to buy more expansions and the large time commitment. Eldritch Horror has some of these things, too. But I'm not about to multiply that times two. 

As to that random element, it creates incredible suspense. That's the kind of game it is. But you can do something about it if you know how to play smart. You can lessen the risk, but it's still there. And that makes the game incredibly exciting and intense! And that leads to a whole lot of fun! To hell what other people think, I love this game! What an awesome treat this game is!

P.S. I own all of the expansions, Forsaken Lore, Mountains of Madness, Strange Remnants, Under The Pyramids,  Signs of Carcosa, The Dreamlands, and Cities in Ruin. I would say that if you want variety that will never get old, buy as many expansions as you can as soon as you can. Don't wait til you're seeing the same cards over and over. Having more different types of cards permanently extends the life of all of your cards. The more cards you have, the more plays you get without seeing the same card. So all cards will never get old.

The greatest thing about this game though is that because the story immersion is good, but not RPG like, you can run across the same card again, and it won't seem like the story has already been spoiled. There's so many choices to make that lie outside the narration on the cards. You have to solve mysteries, fight monsters, and close gates. You'll need to acquire assets, artifacts, and spells to help you do that. The order and way you do that will be based on what the board has set before you and how you choose to mitigate whatever risks you face. And that is the core of the game, which makes it an awfully slick and solid game. The story  narration adds icing on the cake and gives this game flavor as it gives context to all the play mechanics of the game.

The game play mechanics of the game are tight and balanced and are all based on risk management. And that is really what a mystery/adventure horror should be.

RATING: 6.5
I'll agree with BGG's placement for this game in their thematic list because truly this game oozes theme.  It really is the main appeal of the game when you're gazing at the impeccable artwork for all the Old One's cards and devouring the well-written lore spilled on every encounter card.
That being said I will disagree with BGG's placement for this game in their overall list because other than the theme I felt the gameplay was a bit dry and I was surprised at just how much of a dice game this turned out to be.
If you're a sucker for Lovecraftian  mythos or just love a game with thick horror/sci-fi theme then I recommend this game for being very immersive.  However, if you're not a big fan of either then you can safely pass on this as it's very heavy on theme but light on actual game.  
Not to mention holy christ is this game a pain to set up and take down.  That alone makes me rethink pulling this off my shelf considering how light the game feels.

RATING: 8
Pros:
- Flavor text is built into the game to create a neat story that encourages players to fill in the blanks.

- Random card draw everywhere adds to replayability.

- Fun character progression in terms of gaining increasing attributes, items, spells, and artifacts with unique character powers.

- Ancient ones come with unique cards to each enemy. Modular side boards and mechanisms can enhance experience once you get setup down quickly.

- Tokens and cards are good quality with evocative art and have a functional layout with easy to read text and iconography.

Cons: 

- Needs expansions to make it more replayable, but there are a lot to buy

- More players means a longer game unless you start resolving the encounter phase simultaneously when there are no impacts to others' choices. Also less reading of text aloud to share the experience since you need to keep the game moving briskly.

Summary:

Simply one of the best cooperative adventure style games out there with dice rolls for tests and powering up your character to face great evil.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Overall, a very good re-make of Arkham Horror. The changes are really well done, incorporating a lot of very good ideas. The game seems a bit easier than Arkham Horror to win, and it also seems easier to introduce new players to. It still takes a few hours to play (more than what they say on the box) and it does have a problem with scaling (not at all managed well for more players).
But Fantasy Flight has let greed/stinginess ruin what could be a very excellent game. Aside from just being more than blatantly obvious that FF has left themselves more than enough space (a gaping void?) to fill with numerous expansions, they have really skimped on the contents of the core game.  
Aside from the painfully obvious limited replay-ability of the main objectives (Elder Gods), repetition quickly sets in due to the small number of location and item/ally cards. It is common to cycle through all the cards in an area/region, and/or the item deck, more than once (especially if certain characters are played) in a single game. Duplicates of certain items would be nice (especially if they are the starting items of some characters), but more item/condition/spell/location cards in general would be ... are actually really necessary. (For instance, there aren't even enough Blessed or Cursed condition cards to go around in a 4 player game -- and this is a situation that has happened to us more than once.)
The basic/glaring downside of Eldritch Horror is the limited number of game components, for what is supposed to be the major core game. Not screwing their customers on that alone would really have made it a more robust game. The flood of expansions that I'm sure FF has planned may help with replay in terms of the over-arching plots and goals, but I hope they realize that they will also have to provide more basic components to help the regular game-play seem less repetitive. It's a shame they didn't supply more base game components, something to give the game a lifespan of more than 8 or so plays (if only FF hadn't cheaped out on this basic element). 
(I have played the game 4 times now, the obvious areas of repetition are already kind of a (tedious) joke, but replay now involves selecting an Elder God for a 2nd play thru and, if things are optimal, that will be an experience of 66% repetition.)
Unless you can pick this game up cheap (along with a REALLY GOOD expansion or 2), I can't really say that this game is worth it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another great Cthulhu game from Fantasy Flight that lands somewhere between Mansions of Madness and Arkham Horror both in feel and in epicness.  My favorite feature is the way the mysteries and research cards are different for each Ancient One, giving the whole game a slightly different flavor for each AO.

HOWEVER, all this talk about EH "replacing" Arkham Horror is poppycock. Poppycock, hooey and malarkey I say!  EH is a tighter design than AH, but this also means the game is more constrained and presents fewer possibilities.  It is not really all that less fiddly than AH either.  Having to check every game component for reckoning effects makes the Mythos phase almost as much of a hassle as AH.  The influence roll to get items can be just as frustrating as trying to get money to buy items in AH, maybe more so because you can't accumulate successes.  Now you need to track damage on the monsters.  I miss taking the multi-turn journeys through the other worlds.  I also feel like EH splits your attention between tasks more than AH.  You can't just focus on the gates, monsters, and occasional rumors like in AH.  Now you have to deal with the mysteries as well.  And getting across the board can take forevers.  You can't just boost your speed and run for it like in AH.

I think that a lot of the flight to EH from AH comes from people who have overexpanded their AH and lost sanity points as a result.  If you eat yourself sick at the buffet, that's not the buffet's fault, people! :)  

I can see EH being a good option for those who are new to this genre.  EH feels about like AH + the revised Dark Pharoah in complexity.
 
That being said, EH is a great game that reworks the AH premise into a more compact but also less epic format while incorporating some of the features from MoM.  EH, AH, and MoM will all coexist happily on my shelf, and I am very picky about which games get to live on my shelf.

UPDATE Nov 2014:
I've been really enjoying playing this solo. 
I can run 3 characters without trouble, but I'm finding the game very difficult I win this way. While clues are plentiful, "# investigators/2 (rounded up) = 2, which makes the mysteries tough.  I'll try 2 characters next and see if I fare any better. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

RATING: 9
Big Lovecraft fan, and no game has done a Cthulu mythos game as well as Eldritch Horror. I'm excited every time I get to turn over a card. One day, I might even beat Azathoth and move onto a different Old One!

For me, this game fixed my issue with co-operative games. I tend to rank co-operative low, because I tend to mentally shutdown during them as a tactic to avoid Alpha gaming. This results in me being consistently bored through-out the game.  Eldritch Horror cuts through this limitation of mine, but making my own gameplay independently important to the goal. Rather than needing flawless co-ordination, each player carries out their own investigation with bigger-picture objectives.

(Maybe this reduced co-ordination is why we never win... No matter! I'll keep on playing the way that's fun for me and my group!)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This fits into an odd space.  It's not quite as long as Arkham, but almost.  (It's close enough that I don't know that I'd ever want to play this when I wouldn't want to play its sister game.)  It's a little more manageable, and you perhaps feel like you have more control.  More of the information is open, certainly.  I like the fact that the Mythos deck is seeded in stages, so you don't wind up with a bunch of super-hard cards coming out at once.  
Ultimately, though, it's still about the roll of the dice.  In a way, this is worse than Arkham, because almost all of the goals revolve around turning in clue tokens.  In that game, you can just walk around and pick them up, but in this one you have to pass an encounter to do so.  If you have events that fail to put out clues where you can get to them or you just get unlucky at picking them up, the game can slide towards defeat and there's nothing you can do about it.

A lot of the design touches are nice.  Conditions/spells are double-sided and different on the back, so you know that something bad will happen but not exactly what.  Each boss has a way that it affects the game and a stack of missions ('mysteries') that play along with that.  This means games will play out differently instead of being a race to seal gates.
However, the base game decks of encounters and mysteries are awfully thin, so you'll be seeing repeats a lot.

RATING: 8.5
I've only played this 5 times, but so far this fires Arkham Horror. I love that game, but this cuts down on some of the more irritating book keeping aspects (terror track, monster limits, monster moving, character sliders, the upkeep phase in general).

It does this whilst retaining depth and variety. There's a lot to praise, but I'd single the reckoning mechanic out for particular attention. A fantastic and simple idea that adds surprise and tension to every game.

There is plenty of variety in the base set, but you'll quickly want Forsaken Lore. This is mainly for additional encounter cards, in the same way that Arkham required Dunwich.

Buy this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
For the most part, I've moved on from what I call MERSC games (move, explore, roll for skill check) as I look for more sophistication in my solo/coop games. I can see through the veil of the experience rather quickly when all we’re doing is rolling for skill checks with a bunch of cards that modify our rolls. And neither do I want to rely on expansion chasing to keep the experience fresh, but (wait for it)… I like this game and series of expansions.

Admittedly, a big part of the reason is that the genre is a huge attraction for me. I love the [blogpost=27416]Lovecraftian Yog Sothothery[/blogpost] quite a bit and enjoy a game facilitating a story about it. Also, I firmly believe that a Lovecraftian game should be cooperative and not competitive because once you introduce a competitive situation, the theme of inevitable madness, chaos, and futility underlying the genre goes out the window because people want to win. It shouldn’t get anywhere near that but rather everyone enjoying when things going horribly wrong. Can’t argue it’s not thematic!

So, those are my ideological reasons for liking the game, as to the game itself, it’s streamlined to my tastes. EH tells me a story with a modicum of rules and keeps the action flowing without a lot of downtime or management of the AI. I like Mansions of Madness for the way it provides a sense of mystery, and EH offers at least a taste of that with double-sided cards where you get something that may flip over at some point to the consequence side and then you have to deal with that. There’s some intelligence built into the game such that mummies appear at the Pyramids and other monsters act thematically to keep the theater of it all intact. And sometimes the cards just align like finding the Crystal of the Elder Gods at the Temple of the Elder Things (ooh, spooky how that happened!). 

From what I see, some people prefer the added rules chrome of AH, and some of us prefer the streamlining of EH. Rather than argue over which is best, fans of Lovecraft should marvel that we have these choices. Fans of other genres envy how many Cthulhu games there are. Their genres live in shacks while we have mansions (of madness!).

Note: If you don’t have the Mountains of Madness expansion, you still must play with the Focus rules; download the rulebook.

RATING: 4.2
[b]Sold 2017-3-27[/b]  For all the reasons listed below.  I got my fun out of the game for sure, and will still play if friends request with their copies, but this ain't one I need to own anymore.

[b]Updated 2015-12-19[/b] After 14 plays, I am pretty much completely over Eldritch Horror.  "Spend three hours rolling dice to see if you win or not" is just not appealing to me anymore.  The novelty has worn completely off, the story elements are not worth the pain of all the fiddliness, and I pretty much can't wait for games to be over.  I may play it again if I get into a very specific mood, but in general I would be happy to never see this game again.  Downgraded from a 6.7 to a 4

[b]Comments as of 2015-8-14[/b] I have a bit of a love/hate relationship with Eldritch Horror.  I love the epic scale, the grandiose story you tell together with your friends while battling unspeakable horrors.  I love the feeling of starting as a desperate professor or sailor who somehow learned a bit too much, then trying to gear up and learn new skills to try to take on this terrible darkness.

I hate the dice.  Like, really, really hate them.  I hate how you can get everything completely right, make amazing decisions and have things on the verge of victory, and then a bad roll on the most mundane undertaking can totally throw you off kilter.  I hate that, in order to do all of that awesome leveling up, you have to simply roll well for it, and if you spend just a few turns trying to level up but failing your rolls, you're pretty much hosed.

When the dice rolls obey the general trends of probability - that is, you succeed some, but fail more, the game works amazingly well.  It tells a great story of trying to overcome an insurmountable task, and whether you lose or win, you feel like you've traversed the world trying to save it.

When the dice rolls are simply crap, though, the game turns into a boring slog.  You don't feel like you're on an epic quest, you feel like a loser who can't manage to convince somebody to sell you a stinking handgun even though you've tried multiple times.  When you aren't able to get geared up, to take on those epic battles, because you fail at the simplest, most menial of tasks, it feels less like a game and more like an exercise in futility.  It seems like approximately a quarter of my games have gone this way.  I keep bringing Eldritch Horror back to the table knowing that I'll probably have a good time, but when it falls flat, it falls hard.

RATING: 6

RATING: 9.5
Pros
-Better than AH (streamlined play/easier to manage rules, shorter/pre-determined game length, logical win path and actions you take are not made redundant if AO wakes, less bookkeeping, more tension)
-Incorporates MoM style random effects from conditions, spells etc. 
-Allows for teamwork, but also allows for solo play
-Varied game-play / approach to winning depends on Ancient One you are facing.
-(to be verified) Decent difficulty level (I prefer harder games).

Cons
-Alpha player potential problem
-Desperately needs an expansion (barely enough encounter cards in box) - as is, expect somewhat lower long term replayability
-No miniatures despite high price (but otherwise good component quality as per FFG)
-Events are still fairly unpredictable, leading to attribute tests being somewhat random (unless you memorize what is at each location)
-Mysteries for each AO that needs to be completed are somewhat similar. Could have used more innovation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This streamlining of Arkham Horror seems excellent, though some of the decks are a bit thin.

Old Ones have custom mythos deck compositions that change as the game progresses (variety, coherence, and narrative), as well as custom quest cards. Instead of closing gates you have to accomplish special missions to win (though closing gates gains you some respite).

I really like the modularity of the Old Ones - it seems an improvement over the deck dilution issues in Arkham Horror, and I hope FFG can maintain this level of design as the expansions come out.

There are fewer phases, no sliders, money or elder signs, allies and items have been merged, cities state what their encounters tend to do, monsters stay put, and effects only occur on reckonings, which simplifies bookkeeping.

I hope that at least the initial expansions flesh out the existing decks and mechanics rather than adding more.

I agree with some of the criticisms:
* decks are thin
* "Delay" is a stupid lose-a-turn mechanic
* "Shuffle back in a solved mystery" is execrable

RATING: 8
I love this game. From the way it distills its theme, to its simple and fun game mechanics, to its impressive replay value, to its stellar components, everything about Eldritch Horror feels right. The game does a great job at always keeping you on the edge, throwing rumors and opening gates all around the world, so that you always have to make hard and interesting choices. Where the system really shines is in the synergy it creates between adventurers with very different skills and backgrounds. While the muscle will keep the monsters at bay, the brain will investigate and work at solving the mysteries. It's one of the rare games that give me such a sense of immersion without forgetting the fun and strategy. If you like adventure and mystery, Indiana Jones and Lovecraft, then look no further.
