RATING: 2
Whenever the game asks for a skill check, passing or failing is based upon the number of successes in a dice roll where the dice count is dependent upon the individual investigator's skill. While this reduces the frustration of rolling dice to some extent, it does not change that this is an awful dice fest.
On their turns, players effectively move and then they have an encounters on their locations. In order to complete the game, players must chase down clue tokens that generate randomly, which means that in many cases, players are going to sit in one location, trying to win a clue over and over again.
While various Old-Ones are available to pursue and they do change the rules of the game in clever ways, one runes through the number of cards supplied for each within a single game, rendering their variation a little moot as more than a single play results in narrative repetition.
If an Ameritrashian game such as this cannot even immerse its players, what value does it have?

(2 plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An excellent sequel to the already great Arkham Horror.  While it isn't shorter than Arkham, it definitely improves on everything that was already working, while making something that still feels like a brand new game.

Eldritch's best new feature is double sided cards. Conditions and spells have multiple different versions, and certain game effects cause you to flip them over. When they're flipped, something unique to that card happens. It makes spell casting really interesting, and conditions are much more interesting than simply being cursed or blessed.

Another great aspect to the game, is that they made Artifact cards feel MUCH more unique and special. You can't just go to the store and buy artifacts anymore. When you get an artifact from a card effect (usually from entering gates), it is extremely rewarding.

Clues and Gates have been changed to be specific to locations on the game board. This means that you are much more likely to visit varying places in the game, rather than focusing on the locations that are beneficial despite the state of the game (like shops in Arkham). When a clue spawns, you get sent to not just cities, but small points in the middle of nowhere. Gates were further changed to no longer send you to a specific other world, which while slightly disappointing at face value does give you a lot more variety and surprise when you're drawing other world cards as encounters.

Eldritch also does a lot more to make the game feel unique to each elder god you face. Each god has it's own unique deck that you have encounters with to collect clues. However, these decks only have 8 cards, and over the course of the game you WILL go through that deck 3-4 times. The game is in desperate need for more variety in these elder god decks. Each card does have 3 different effects on it (City, wilderness, and sea) similar to Arkham having 3 effects per card for locations, but there still isn't enough variety in a single game. 

Unlike Arkham, where the focus is mostly on closing gates, the actual focus of Eldritch is solving mysteries. Each god has 4 mysteries, 3 of which must be solved to win the game. Mysteries give you unqiue uses for clues, or require you to time actions in the game with what "Omen" is currently in play.

Aside from the need for more elder god specific cards to add variety during a game, the biggest flaw of Eldritch is that some game effects cause you to "lose" an already solved mystery. This is by far the WORST mechanic any game could consider adding. It is one thing to have effects in the game that make it harder or that cause stress to the players, but actually losing progress is the complete opposite of fun. Especially when that "progress" is 1/3 of the entire game. I don't care if the game was balanced to expect you to complete 4 mysteries instead of 3, having one get stolen from you made us nearly want to quit the game. In our first game we went from 1/3 complete and starting on our second mystery, to being back to square one. At that point, we would have been better off quitting and restarting the game entirely.

The game is ripe for Expansions, and was clearly created with expansions in mind.  First and foremost, I really hope that the 4 elder god decks get more variety. Beyond that, more elder gods, more players, and more cards in general would be a huge boost to an already great game.


Edit: After playing a few more times, an overlooked rule has made the repetition of the research deck much less obvious. Originally we mixed clues back into the supply each time it was used, which meant we had duplicate clues showing up in the same game. This also meant that there were always more city clues in the supply than everything else, meaning when we went on research encounters we were seeing the same ones.

Once following the rules that state clues get discarded, and only put back in the supply when you run out, we found it much less likely to repeat the same exact encounters. There still needs to be more variety in the research decks, but it's not nearly as bad as it was during our first impression.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've been playing a lot of this.  It really does solve a lot, but not all of, AH's problems.  In particular, it makes everything important again---you no longer pile up a thousand useless things that barely work (e.g. spells), even individual clues are important, die rolls never get to a point where they required 12 die rolls and cascades of successes.  It also rebalanced the characters better and sets a stronger balance between the utility of different attributes for tests.  My essential take on the AH vs. EH argument is that AH creates amazing unintended narrative that elevates it to an amazing story creator---but those stories are almost always micro stories about characters and situations and sometimes incoherent.  EH, by contrast, is a very intentional macro story creator for the entire game state.

Obviously the number one thing it does, which is succeeds at, is implementing card decks and details that make GOOs play differently.  It must have been a bit of a hard decision to devote 1/3 of the cards in the product to GOOs, only one of which will see play.  But it was the right decision.

I still have some issues with the game that I'm mulling over.  Monsters are both unimportant now but also endless, stacking up 3+ high frequently all over the board.  Multiple round combat makes things less random, but can also create a lot of frustrating delay as you grind monsters for multiple rounds.  Gates are difficult to close  (2+ successful tests, clues) but pop up at a rate that makes their closure seem/feel worthless.

So I've calibrated this at 8.5.  It may move up or down.  I don't think it'll ever hit the 10 with me that AH is, because its high highs aren't even close to the sublime, fun situations AH can put you in.  On the other hand, its lows aren't as low either.

Also, Forsaken Lore is essentially a requirement if you buy the base game and like it at all.

Edit: This game is dropping.  It's an 8 now down from a high of 9.5 in my 10-15 play honeymoon.  There is some serious unfun gotcha bullshit in this game on the mythos cards, tons of delay mechanics, etc.  This is a crying shame because mechanically, besides the reduction of dice thrown for checks making the game more random, this is a considerable improvement over AH.  But someone on the design team just has super lazy, unfuriating ideas for mythos cards and encounter punishments.

RATING: 9.5
The most elegant mechanics and immersive theme of the FFG Lovecraftian games. Most of my plays have been solo with 4 investigators. EH reduced some of the Arkham Horror gameplay complexity regarding acquisition of resources and monster encounters while creating an AO-based story line from tailored mysteries, clue encounter and special encounters.  The emphasis on global encounters lacks some of town or regional character intimacy of Arkham (but is still representative of Lovecraft stories such as "Call of Cthulhu," "Shadow Out of Time," "At the Mountains of Madness" and others, as well as non-Lovecraft Mythos tales, such as as Bloch's Egypt stories and Howard's "The Black Stone").  This one has improved with its expansions and the proliferation of all materials , particularly a more diverse array of location encounters and investigator tools.  A great game, and a more elegant game than Arkham Horror, but not yet quite as rich.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I don't own a lot of good tabletop games, but this one is perfect for me hence the score. I'm a big fan of Lovecraft's works and obsessed with the 20s so I love the theme and story of this game. I got into board gaming over a year ago but haven't been tempted to get a new game yet, choosing instead to buy expansions for it because it's so fun. I own all expansions except for Signs of Carcosa which I should be getting in a few days.

The best aspect of this game is its great theme but I also like the strategy of it. When playing alone for hours its pretty intense as I study every action and possibility to see if and how I can win. All my 10 plays are solo except the most recent one which I played with my family. They're not board gamers so they didn't exactly get the hang of it but also didn't notice a lot of strategy so don't get it if you want it purely for that. It's a very competent solo game, I always play with 4 investigators now. This game is more story than strategy though so I imagine it would be very fun to play with friends who may like Lovecraft or games like this to have a laugh and make things more interesting.

As a word of warning, a good portion of encounters is just luck. You can gamble and strategize to have the odds be in your favor but sometimes the dice just won't let you win. Take our most recent game with 3 players as an example. An already hard and odd number of investigators, we drew the All for Nothing mythos (shuffle solved mystery back in) without the clues to stop it. We were able to solve three mysteries total and were so close to the last. We were playing Azathoth and the final mystery required us to discard six clues total in Tunguska after an observation test. We let the gates go because we thought we'd win before the doom got to 0 but nope. For two turns in a row I attempted the observation test (with Joe Diamond no less) plus an ally and a reroll the second time for a total of 11 dice rolled hoping for a single 1/3 chance success. Didn't get it either time and lost with nothing we could do to change the result further. That stuff stinks but it was still an interesting conclusion to a fun game.

With all the money I've spent on it I still get an average of $5 per hour of gameplay which is better than a lot of media. That number is higher than it should be though as I've taken long breaks between sessions and only play one or a couple sessions before buying another expansion, to the point where I haven't even fought two AOs and haven't beaten half of them.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Good modular story driven american style game with dice roling.
1st game - 2 pl. - Azathoth - win - about 180 mins incl. Setup - fun
 Even with just two players the game is long, therefore I think I would refuse to play it with more than three players.  Playing EH with the max. amount of 8 players would result in ridiculously high downtime!
Having wrote this;
2nd game - 6 pl - Cthullhu - lost - 240mins - horrible
Yes, as assumed downtime becomes a big fun killer. Furthermore as long as you wait another player may trigger an effect that you lose some health or sanity (felt strange). Therefore our investigators died more than once.... Another fun killer.
3rd game - 3 pl - Yig - win - 180mins - 2hrs fun, last hour dragged a bit.

In general I like this game. But you can be "stoppped"  meaning you lose a turn, this may also happen two times in a row.   Losing a turn just means that the game will go on for longer so this is a very cumbersome mechanism that does nothing but weaken the fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Thus far the best coop game I've played. I love the globe-trotting, Indiana Jones experience. I love the encounter phase where something different happens every time. I love the different investigators, the standees, all of the equipment, spells and conditions you can get. I love the Cthulhu theme and the 1920-30's jazzy setting. Above all I really love that you can easily play this solo, justifying (to a degree) all the money I've spent on this game.

The component quality is great, especially the linen finish on the cards.

Two aspects of this game that you have to accept: the randomness and the duration of a game. The game has a lot of dice rolling with few ways to mitigate the luck factor, aside from rolling more dice. On top of that the Mythos deck can either make the game quite easy or super difficult when build using the standard rules. As for the duration: I have only played 1 game of EH that was under 3 hours. Every other game has been around 4 hours, not including setup. 

Issues: 
- lack of dice: the game only comes with 4 dice which is just way too few
- no bag or cup for the monster and clue tokens. You need to provide these yourself so you can randomly draw one when the game asks you to. 
- no insert and not enough plastic bags, so storage is a problem
- without expansions the base game will feel repetitive after roughly 5/6 plays

All of these problems can be fixed with money. I've bought extra dice, monster and clue bags, build my own insert and own all of the expansions. As a minimum I think you have to invest in plastic bags, dice and buy the Forsaken Lore expansion.

The one thing I've felt missing from Eldritch Horror that money can't buy is closure. When you solve a mystery or beat an Ancient One nothing happens. There is no text to read, nothing that celebrates your success. I don't expect music or balloons, a few lines of story would suffice. Sadly this is completly lacking so you just have to make it up yourself. 

I will wholeheartedly recommend Eldritch Horror, just not the naked base game.

RATING: 9
Gameplay is something between Battlestar Galactica (in terms of the way the turn is structured), Pandemic (in the global movement and goals), and Arkham Horror (in event and conflict resolution).  Intricate and complex gameplay mitigated by an easy to understand rulebook that keeps the choices a player can make relatively straightforward.  Not short by any means, but would probably scale well with different numbers of players.  Really looking forward to playing this one again soon.

RATING: 8
This is a great game, somewhere between Elder Sign (which is easy to play and learn) and Arkham Horror or Mansions of Madness (which are tough to get to the table). Very richly thematic, with just enough chaos to be unpredictable but not demoralizing. The feeling of travelling the globe is palpable, and the Big Bad Guys you're fighting against each give the game a different feel. This may be the best Lovecraftian board game out there, very well balanced, tightly designed, and fun even for people who aren't fans of other Lovecraft-themed games.

RATING: 7.7
No other game gets me quite as invested as Eldritch Horror. This was the third board game I ever bought, and the first one that showed me just what the medium was capable of. This game takes you on a journey, sometimes dark and  grueling, sometimes silly and absurd. It is stressful and delightful. It can deliver powerful, memorable narratives, and also spin nonsensical yarns. The game is far from flawless and is, at it's core a series of "fetch quests" determined by dice rolls. The game plays you more than you play it, but my goodness is it a fun and wild ride. Whenever I have 4 hours open in my schedule, I am begging my housemates to join me for another adventure. Sadly, that does not happen too often, otherwise, this game would be rated higher for me.

Cons: A mighty game, think 3+hrs with 2 players, but it does have an epic feel to it. It can be a bit daunting, with so many cards and so many bits. You also need to buy a expansion or two, as the you quickly cycle through the location cards.

This is a great game, but one that should not be played too often. Think, once a month maybe, or once very two month.

RATING: 10
[b]Stuff to do before world ends:[/b]

√ [i]Party at Hearst Castle[/i]
√ [i]Party at Alcatraz[/i]
√ [i]Strangle a wizard[/i]

[microbadge=3392]

This is my favorite kind of game, a [i]choose-your-own-adventure[/i] book cut up and pasted onto cards.*

[size=7]*For best effect, have the left-hand player draw and read the card for the active player—in dramatic fashion—while keeping the pass/fail results secret.  Only tell them the choices, and then once made and rolled-for, the resulting outcome—don't give away any results they didn't get.  This keeps things [i]mysterious[/i].  (It's best for the left-hand player to do this as the right-hand player may still be doing bookkeeping while finishing up their turn, and it gives everyone a rotating slot as storyteller.[/size]

Now that we've played enough to get the rhythm of the thing I quite like it, especially the feeling of putting your own team together, via allies, and taking on different aspects of the mega-plot while coordinating with other globe-trotting groups.  You can almost see the red arrows gliding from place to place while the adventure-travel music beats a tattoo of ominous progress.

The mechanical stuff's fine, I guess—I don't really notice it, which says a lot—but so far the emergent narratives have been fantastic.  Which is all I really care about.

So, does this replace [gameid=15987]?  Not really.  They're two different beasts.  [i]Arkham[/i] is a mechanically quirky and convoluted thing (facets I find charming) about three-fisted Mythos adventures in New England, while [i]Eldritch[/i] is the cleaner, tighter, "more modern" global version.  They're different enough to be distinct and enjoyed for the unique experiences they provide.

RATING: 7
This is essentially Arkham Horror 1.5 - the same game, more or less, but with some streamlining and a change of focus from a city-based setting to a globe hopping one.

The streamlining won't survive the inevitable bloat from wave after wave of expansions (and I prefer the crunch of Arkham in any case) but the major problem with the design ethos is that the global setting offers far less narrative focus and more of an Indiana Jones pulp vibe in my opinion. There's not a lot of Lovecraft left in FFG's Lovecraftian horror at this point.

The game ships with too few cards - you really need the Forsaken Lore expansion if you don't want to see the same cards come up twice in the same game.

Having said all that, this is still a really enjoyable and engaging co-op and it's great for solo gaming too (table space allowing). If the superior Arkham Horror didn't exist, I'd probably really rate this game.

RATING: 6
It is a great Adventure, but somehow, the things you can do are very limited. The choice of your character basically tells you how to play the game, and it feels that once you know how to play a character, you are in autoplay mode. That is: You decide at the start who specializes in what, and after that there are not a lot of strategic decisions. Somehow, other popular Coops like Pandemic have more decision making in a shorter playtime. It just doesn't make sense to do things your character has bad attributes in because you are just losing a turn and get punished while doing so. As an example, playing Charlie Kane (the Politican) has two things that are just best: Have Encounters in Tokyo to Kill Monsters and put that Influence to use to buy Assets for everyone. 80 % of the Time, your character can and should be played like that. That doesn't mean it is dumb, because Character Selection is very important, but after that, you have a very narrow corridor.

Don't get me wrong, I LOVE variable Player Powers. You take a game like Imperial Assault and your Brain is going to hurt from all the Options you have, even though your character might be bad at some things. 

That beeing said, there is a lot of fun story in it, and I think my first two playthroughs this game was a 9 for me.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The best designed game in its class. Shows Arkham Horror up as the ancient terror that it is, for my money EH is better in every conceivable way. And Flying Frog's versions of the same thing? Fuhgedaboutem!

The action system is simple and straight forward and keeps the game moving. 

The encounter system is great, the best in any game I can think of. The encounters are thematic, and simple to resolve, and the two types, simple and complex, mean even more variety.

The Mythos phase adds just the right amount (usually) of complication and misery, without the excessive arbitrariness you get in games like Fortune and Glory or Touch of Evil.

The use of decks specific to each Ancient One keeps everything feeling relevant and thematic, while the double sided condition, spell and (with expansions) asset cards mean you're never quite sure what's going to happen when a certain card comes into play. Even if you've been poisned before, the effects of this particular poisoning are uncertain, until you flip the card. It's very cool.

I think the word that best describes EH is 'tuned'. Everything feels like it's been thought through and tweaked to be just right. The Mythos deck for example. You construct it differently for each Ancient One, so they can all have slightly different feels. And there are three different types of card, and you combine them in a certain ratio, so the deck will have just the right mix of different types of screwage. AND they have difficulty levels, so you can tweak your game to be easier or harder, or get progressively more difficult, just as you wish.

The expansions integrate very intelligently with the base game, adding more content to the standard encounter decks which is relevant to any game, and a variety of stuff, sideboards, extra decks, that you can choose to use or not. Adding a sideboard is as simple as getting out the decks related to it and adding a couple of tokens to the relevant pools, hardly adds any set up time at all.

It's just great, excellently done, maybe the best designed FFG game there is?

Even so, there are a ridiculous number of decks of cards in the game, especially once you start adding in expansions, and that's not going to be for everybody. 

It is for me though, bring on the madness!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
It's a good game that can sometimes be great, and other times bog down. I love the theme, and the board and all of the components. It does a great job at instilling that sort of eerie early 20th century vibe into each game. The use of a "doom" track (a clock that triggers a sort of "new" game if it runs down)is fantastic for creating that desperate feeling of racing against time to defeat these unnatural forces of terror. I also like that the game is TOUGH, and that sometimes there's just not much one can do to affect the outcome of the game at certain junctures. It does a great job at facilitating the feeling that we as players are out of our element fighting against forces that are beyond our comprehension...

However, this is also one of the game's biggest faults.  Because when that system works, it is a fantastically thematic game experience, but when it doesn't work, the game just becomes a literal exercise in futility and can really demoralize a group into defeatist thinking. The game ceases to be thematically engaging at that point, and instead draws too much attention to the underlying mechanics as to make it unenjoyable. This would not be a problem for a shorter game, but games of Eldritch Horror can be up to 4 hours long. 

However, good long games of Eldritch Horror are wonderful and make people want to play again right away even though they know the time commitment required. It's also great that it's a true co-op (without needing someone to play as "the game"). I must say that if I'm going to play a long, dense horror game, I would choose Mansions of Madness over Eldritch Horror every time. But Eldritch Horror definitely has its place, and I enjoy bringing it out from time to time.

RATING: 7.5
2015-06-08

Initial Rating: 7.5 (June 2015)

I love Arkham Horror and Eldritch Horror is very similar, so I also liked it a lot. As with my first AH play, the first EH play was plagued with many rules questions, (I didn’t have the time to read the rules before the game) but it was very fun.

The rules can be explained under 20 minutes and playtime is around 3 hours. The game components and art are great as is usual from FFG.

I can´t help but compare both games:

•	The theme is the same in both games (obviously), but the mechanics in Eldritch Horror are streamlined and easier to grasp, however I’m not sure if EH offers a better game experience. Unless most of the games I play, I do love this theme.

•	The investigator´s skill set will remain static for most of the game, so it makes the game quicker. In Arkham Horror, I really liked to tweak my investigators abilities each turn, furthermore, as they are opposites it was a hard decision. I’m still not sure which way I prefer.

•	The playtime is shorter in Eldertich Horror.

•	The monsters are quite similar, but the combat is very different. We no longer fight to the death in each combat match, also we don’t have the possibility of evade anymore. In my only game, I felt the monsters aren’t that important, they mostly are relevant if you finish your movement on them (they no longer restrict movement) and many times we could ignore them (with some exceptions). I liked more the way Arkham Horror handled them, they were the source of much tension because they moved and many hunted you. The Epic monsters are great, from the art, to the different component shape (so you can easily find them). There are no longer trophies nor terror track. 

•	The movement is similar (Point to Point), but you can only move once each turn and then use your travel tickets. We no longer have a speed stat. I like this because you have to think were in the world you want to go ahead of time. In general, you cannot travel quickly from one place to another. This also makes thematic sense as you are traveling the world and not a city or town.

•	The gates are less convoluted, but I miss the way they were handled in Arkham Horror, now they are as any other encounter in the game and have an unique deck for all of them, so it no longer matters where the gate connects to (a shame). Also, in my only play, a strong physical character had a very low possibility of closing the game because all tests were made with Lore, not strength. This makes perfect thematic sense so I approve. This also makes much more important to have a rounded group of investigators. The gate encounters are a series of tests which can be the equivalent of the encounters of the other worlds in AH, so I also approve of this. Closing gates continues to be relevant, if you don’t the Ancient One will awake quickly.

•	The difficulty of the base game is harder in Eldritch Horror. One of my main complaints about Arkham Horrar was that it was too easy to beat (but the expansions fix this).  

• 	In Eldritch Horror you need to solve mysteries to win and if the Ancient One awakes you have to solve another mystery. This is very thematic and I love the way EH handles it. It has a different deck for each Ancient One. 

•	In Eldrith Horror you have much less clue tokens. This makes the game a lot harder because you cannot re roll as often as before and getting the clue tokens is also much harder. I’m still not sure about this because without Clue Tokens you are at luck´s mercy, but I also like to have a harder game.

•	I love the way Eldritch Horror manages the stores, now it does thematic sense and you can (or try to) buy whatever you want, not at random as Arkham Horror handled it.

•	Now it is an official rule that the active player won’t read his encounters and won’t know the other option if he passes or fail a test. I love this and it makes the game much more enjoyable for me. This is also the way we played Arkham Horror before.

•	Eldritch Horror has a plethora of conditions. I think this is the legacy of Dunwitch Horror and I approve. I love that they have hidden information on them and you only look when a condition is met. This is also how the spells works.

•	In Eldrtich Horror when an investigator is defeated he dies and he is out of the game for good (the player will restart with another investigator, there is no player elimination unless the Ancient One is awake). I love this because it makes perfect sense, also you can search the investigator remains to pick up his equipment :D.
•	The fight with the Ancient One is heavily modified, now if it awakes it makes the game harder for the investigators, but there isn’t an instantaneous fight with it, I also like this change.

•	The Mythos cards now form a limited deck, this a huge plus because it sets a clock for the game to finish and it doesn’t drag. It also adds a source of tension for the instigators (they lose if depleted) and, as each Ancient One makes his own mythos deck, it also serves as a difficulty setting. I really like this change.

•	In general, Eldritch Horror is easier to manage but I found it to be less in many respects. It has less components, less cards (most of the decks were combined, there are no longer ally decks, common item decks etc. They are all combined), less gates. However this doesn´t mean Eldritch Horror is less of a game, in fact I loved it. I think I prefer Arkham Horror simply because I played it first but I’m worried EH has now a low replay value (it only come with 4 Ancient Ones). I’m sure the expansions will fix this but AH came with 8 Ancient Ones right from the start.

Bottom line, I love the game and I will buy all its expansions. At this stage in my gaming career (5 years into the hobby), I’m liking euros more and more, however Eldritch Horror will always have a place in my collection along with Arkham Horror, for me it isn’t a replacement. I want to play it again soon. 

Current Rating: 8.0

2016-01-27

At the end of last year, I noticed a change in my gaming tastes. I’m drifting away from Ameritrash games. I still enjoy a lot of them, but my desire to play them is, generally, very low. There are exceptions (like Eldritch Horror) but overall I’m getting bored by them. This new trend has affected most Ameritrash games including this one, so I’m downgrading its rating. Still Eldritch Horror is a game that has a permanent spot in my game collection.

RATING: 10
I would be lying if I do not admit that when I saw so many cards being seperated out into many different piles, I was going, '...choo choo fiddle train coming right up!' But when I actually set it up and started playing, everything just clicked right in. Even on my very first game, I only had to reference the rule book maybe 3-4 times, and did not feel like I missed any major rules. This game is wonderfully streamlined - action phase, encounter phase then mythos phase. Simple.

Every encounter really drew me right into the game. Granted, they felt a bit disjointed as one encounter to next one really did not connect with each another (they do not have to, but if they did, it would have brought this game to another level) but Ancient One's mystery cards tied everything up into nice experience. I agree with 'Drive Thru' review where he commented about how the game became more thematic with each plays as if the Ancient Ones are trying repeatedly to consume this world and we are time and time again trying to stop that from happening. 

The reckoning mechanisms are very well done in this game. It is like that of Robinson Crusoe where event gives you a something nice, but later one it will come back to haunt you. I mean, you pledge a solemn vow to accept super powerful ancient blade, but you will gain a dark pact condition that you will have to pay a hefty price later? I just loved how thematic this mechanism worked. 

The double sided cards (Spells/Conditions) deserves a mention. The fact that your spells can have a varying degree of effects or side-effects depending on your roll test was remarkable.

Another hit for me was how each Ancient One felt so different! Each one had a different set of mystery and research cards that give whole new experience from one another. The base game comes with only 4 Ancient Ones to fight but since mystery cards and research cards are all randomized along with different mythos deck - each play will offer something totally new to the table.

However, the base game desperately needs more encounter cards; I can already see dozens of expansions coming up on the horizon but I wish that they would have included maybe twice more encounter cards to increase the farther variety. Mere 8 encounters per each section of map just did not seem to make much sense to me - and ruins the experience somewhat if you get to see the same encounter again and again in a single game, or remember each encounters from one game from next since there are so few.

The investigators seem very specialized - now this is not a bad thing for most of the part, but this can lead to I will just do this one thing for entire game problem. I mean, there aren't that much fun to be had in this game when you just sit in a city, just having encounters and send your goods to other people while they get to move around and fight monsters. Or, maybe some people actually like doing that so this will be a toss up.

Overall, fantastic game. I cannot wait to play again, and this one will stay on my table for a long time before I get tired of it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
I like, but don't love a Cthulhu theme. I like the vintage, almost steampunk aspects, but find the placement of fantasy demons somewhat wrong. 

I also love co-operative games. Working as a team to beat the game is good fun, and removes the tension of a winner vs loser scenario (in which the loser is oft angry at their play failures (or angry at the game balance/luck), while the victor is either fist pumping or in an awkward 'British' state of trying to make the loser feel better, but making them feel worse with every sentence). Eldritch nicely side-steps these issues.

I'm not a big fan of this game. To me there is something wrong with it. There are too many artificial elements - clues, sub-adventures etc that don't fit with the main plotline. Far too much luck in dice rolling. 

I enjoy the initial choice of characters, I love the mix of brute force and clever (and how clever character like Charlie Kane, Jacqueline Fine etc. seem to better the out and out warriors). I also like the character development - new weapons, injuries and debts. This is one of the games joys, allowing players to really get into character.

Players have some choices, but mostly over where to go, or how best to spend a sub-efficient second action when there is nothing to really use it on (e.g. what to do when in a wilderness you need stay in for your encounter phase). Beyond movement choices are limited, luck takes over. The game leads the players, the players are led by the game. This does not lead to fun game play. Once the storyline with it's seemingly endless mini-events starts to bore and the game starts to drag, many people begin looking for ways to end the game.

Adding players seems to significantly add to the game length. With 5+ players this game can eat hours upon hours. And while the first hour is normally filled with laughs and smiles, the subsequent ones seem to become increasingly subdued as players stop bothering with flavor text and skip to the test icons. 

26/10/2014 - downgrading from initial score of 7 (last night this game bored people for 4 hours, after which we had completed less than 1/4 of the game. Switching to Galaxy Trucker the effect was obvious, smiles, laughs and fun were restored to the whole group.)

RATING: 7
More skillcheckalicious Lovecraftian horror from FFG. This is VERY similar to Arkham Horror, but streamlined. Which should you get? If you liked the idea of Arkham Horror but thought that it was too much work for what you got, you should try this. If what you love about AH is all of the variety and exploration, then EH might feel a bit skimpy to you and there is no need to switch. If you haven't gotten either, may as well start here. It is newer and will be support longer, most likely.

Personally, I'm in the first category and haven't played Arkham Horror in a long time as a result.

If you like drawing cards and taking skill checks then great! That's really just about all there is to these games...

RATING: 8
This is a marvellous melding of theme with contemporary co-op Euro game. If future plays are equally enjoyable I wouldn't be surprised if I increase my rating to 9.

I played 3 games of this back-to-back-to-back. They were all two player games against a different Ancient One each time. Needless to say I was very impressed with the game, we don't often play the same game twice in a row, let alone three times. 

The rules are very straight forward and the underlying mechanics are well implemented. Our games were surprisingly fast the longest was 1 hour and 40 minutes which saw us draw out the entire Mythos deck. Our fastest game was a little over an hour.

The only thing I'm hesitant about is the variety in the Ancient Ones. They have only 4 mystery cards, 3 of which you see each game. The other decks all have plenty of cards. I have yet to play the same Ancient One twice so perhaps this concern isn't warranted.

RATING: 7
Rules and game structure are streamlined compared to Arkham Horror. Completing the mystery card tasks has a Lord of the Rings LCG vibe and gives the game more focus. It’s still a long game but this is probably necessary to evoke the intended atmosphere. At times, I got a little bit bored by the repetitious mechanisms and long times of not accomplishing anything. To be fair, this might be credited to an unlucky streak of getting cursed, failing the next encounter with the consequence of having to pause the next round and getting jailed in the consecutive round. In a five-player game, a lot was happening in the meantime with me being merely a spectator. OTOH, some might see this as a unique gaming experience.

Still, as a cooperative game with lots of theme it’s enjoyable occasionally with the right casual group of players. I have to play Arkham Horror again to make a judgement if I like any of the two games better or if Eldritch can replace Arkham.

RATING: 7
Too early to tell yet from one play, so "7." I'm impressed by the way in which this, in effect, redesign of Arkham Horror managed either to reduce rules complexity or, more impressively, move the complexity to the cards and tokens rather than the rules themselves. The "flavor" is actually pretty good, and the globetrotting approach consistent if not with HPL then with the Call of Cthulhu roleplaying game (e.g., Masks of Nyrlathotep) that is the board game's true progenitor. I think FFG may, though, have maxed out on card and component variety, and the sheer amount of "stuff" makes this game still an every-once-in-a-while affair, with Elder Sign still serving as the default short(er)-game standard in this milieu.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Strictly from game mechanic perspective, it is definitely a great improvement to Arkham Horror. Cleared out a lot of clunky rules and reduce the amount of upkeep work you need per round. 

Unfortunately, I still dislike how little "Game" its provide. As your actions are very limited and the game priorities are quite strictly constrained (If you don't do this within X turns, you will lose), most of the time the player doesn't really make much decision. 

Also, I found that there are quite a bit of snowball effect, Good or Bad roll in the first 3-4 turn can often dictate how easy or hard the game is.

RATING: 9.25
After teetering on the fence for a couple of months on whether or not to get this, I decided to go ahead and give it a chance.  Now, I have never played Arkham Horror due mainly to the fact that by now, it has so many expansions that it would cost hundreds of dollars to surmise the entire collection and I have a distinct need to collect everything relating to a theme that I am interested in.  When I read that Eldritch Horror was a brand new standalone board game that was a streamlined, more intuitive game than its predecessor, I decided that it might be a great alternative.  

I am absolutely thrilled that I gave it a chance!  This game is a complete and total blast to play.  I have only played three games up to now, but each one has been a distinctively unique adventure that spans the world and takes me into crazy encounters with Lovecraft's lore.  I think my favorite aspect of this game is the Choose Your Own Adventure vibes it gives off, letting players decided exactly where they want to go and what they want to do.  For example, I was playing with Leo Anderson the Expedition Leader and found myself exploring the Heart of Africa and deep jungles of the Amazon most of the game while on the other side was Trish Scarborough the Spy, who loaded up on weapons and allies to fight terrifying monsters.  Eventually, these two adventurers met up in Buenos Aires and Leo handed over all the immensely powerful artifacts he discovered to Trish, making her an unstoppable agent against the supernatural darkness.

Overall, this game is really intriguing, diverse, and immensely entertaining.  It's the best solo game I've ever played and it's great with other people too (if you you don't mind explaining the rules and synopsis.)  I will definitely be a dedicated fan to the future content and expansions that Fantasy Flight produces, and I look forward to my future adventures in Eldritch Horror.

RATING: 9
As a newcomer to coop board games this one proved to be a bit of a challenge at first. The first few times I played I was constantly checking the rules (and the handy quick guide included with the game), but after playing a few times with my BF we really got the hang of it and were able to fully utilize the many components of the game. EH can get fairly complicated so having a grasp on the basic flow of the game will help when conditions, artifacts, spells, etc. add further complexity. I will say that this game takes some time to get used to and you really have to get the right group together to play. Be prepared to lose and maybe even sacrifice your character for the sake of winning. My best advice for EH: be adventurous and flexible. This game doesn't always have large rewards for big risks, but it is worth taking a hit or two to finally win your first game. Regardless of winning or losing, my favorite thing about EH is the story. Each of the 8 characters has a unique set of powers that complement their back story, and nearly every card in the game has some sort of flavor text that builds up the suspense. This is a fun game to lose yourself in and I definitely recommend giving it a try.   

Things to thing about when consider when playing/purchasing this game:

Read the rule book completely as you set up the game for the first time. I know that this is hard to do, but trust me it will provide you with a much better experience. 

Know that this game is trying to kill you. If you play like your life depends on it you will have a lot of fun. You might die trying, but hey, that's why you get to start over with a new character. 

Be flexible. You may have to start as a new character mid game. You may have to face an unknown threat. You may become cursed or hurt. In any case, flexible strategy in a must for EH with each game proving to be a unique horror of its own.

07-30-2014 - Talked to Launius about EH vs. AH at Dice Tower and he said he still likes AH better.  After a couple more plays, I think I see what he means.  It's true that EH solves stagnation problems.  It's true that EH is shorter and easier to keep track of.  It might even be more thematic with all the special cards for each GOO.  And I do like the interaction between players and the stressful die rolls that come up with Condition cards and such.  But, in the end, after another play, it's just not the same epic experience that AH is.  And I don't want to weigh EH against AH, they really are different games, but all the same I just feel that as great as EH is, it's not fantastically amazing.  At the risk of becoming cursed, EH goes down to a 9.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6
Played Solo and with 2 Players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Summary: This is a game you really can't appreciate a in single play or without some modification unless you are in a campaign with very experienced players. 

Once you're not looking up every rule on every roll and have tweaked a few things to your liking, it becomes a highly repeatable choose-your-own adventure story with a cast of engaging, sympathetic characters. However, the flavor text that drives the game quickly grows stale, so your love of the setting and mechanics are key.

***

Initial Play Comment: 

The set-up is more daunting than anything. Having divided the tokens and cards based on the set-up map in the manual made it pretty easy to put things on the table. The hardest parts were figuring out what each card was and what location they were bound for. Play was slow in the first three rounds and then more brisk. It felt like my Action phase wasn't good for very much. 

Also, the constant dice-rolling is annoying. It adds too much random number generation to an otherwise story-based game. At minimum I'd prefer some less-random version or at least a die with symbols instead of numbers. At maximum, I'd like some character-based store of roll-values for checks that I can expand and recycle, which adds a strategy element to what to use and when. Maybe this is the D&D player in me, but I also felt there should sometimes be a bonus pass condition for rolling multiple 6s.

***

Plays 2-3 Comments (w/Forsaken Lore and Mountains of Madness w/no sideboard)

Once you understand the turn order, the game runs like clockwork. If you get stuck anywhere, the glossary unsticks you. Actions are brisk, so you don't mind spending some time lingering on the stories told by the cards. I added a set of 1-3-2 style D6 icon die to remove the constant number rolling, and it immediately made me focus more on passing things than getting sixes. 

Despite the ease of game play, two things are just killing my enjoyment. One is trivial - the card management aspect. For both Items and Spells to both be random draw AND a place to go fishing for specific pieces is hugely annoying. The other issue is more serious.

After losing a pair of games I found I didn't mind the loss, but I did mind the lack of agency we had as players. Ancient One mysteries required spells, but neither of us had a means to get them. We pulled several ongoing Events in a row that restricted our rolls and rests and had no way to pick up spells.

After a while, all those brilliant mechanics don't count for much.

***

Plays 4+ Comments (all expansions, but selectively withholding the sideboard or other special mechanics):

It's amazing what some organization and atmosphere does for the enjoyment of this game. 

Organization - A Plano 23600-01 box fits all the tokens and dice (I left out investigators, a tight fit). Cards should be divided based on use - keep the Ancient Ones divided for easy access (I went for 2-each in ziplocks, but ideally they'd each be stored on their own), and Conditions can also be kept segmented since you hardly ever pull a random condition (when you do, just roll to establish which condition type you get). I then organized items between the EH and MoM boxes by things I'll need all game every game (Items, Artifacts) and things I dispose of after set-up (other Investigators and Ancient Ones, AO cards, mythos cards).

This makes set-up a breeze - pick your Ancient One and Investigator, build the mythos deck, and resolve starting conditions. The table is quickly cleared. Go! It takes us less than 15m even with investigator haggling - without, it would be 5m or less.

Atmosphere is a bit harder, but the more we mind it the more fun the play experience. Chiefly, we each bought "Fudge Dice," which are effectively D3s marked with symbols instead of numbers. They're suitable for most rolls in the game aside from the spare number check. Without a focus on numbers, it felt less like a spreadsheet in action and more like a story.

We also stopped reading "Pass" and "Fail" and just continue the story. The "Check Skill" icons became "rely on your X" or "use your Y" instead of telling people to check. We play some instrumental music, either period to Lovecraft or a movie soundtrack. And, finally, we're encouraged to just make stuff up if we hit an Encounter, Mythos, or Gate flavor text we've recently read.

That last point is a big one - this game is sorely lacking in variety in a few key cards you will be using the most. Mythos cards, especially, get cycled through at a clip of 12-16 per game, and you'll quickly have them memorized despite the random mix. Similarly, standard City encounters happen enough that none will be surprising after a few plays. The place where variety excels is with the options for each Ancient One (unique clues and mysteries for each) and the flipped text of Conditions and Spells (meaning no two cards are truly alike, always leaving you with a surprise in play).

From there, you have a potentially smooth game-play experience, but the final aspect is it's just not a fun game with fewer than 3 investigators. Their abilities to interact and save each other's butts make the game fascinating, and just a pair of them isn't enough to do the trick. That means in a two player game you should draft a third investigator and have the Lead chose his/her actions each round - or, simply play with two investigators each.

The difference is the game going from the 4-5 range to the 7-9 range. It's still hard and random and sometimes disappointing, but now it feels like something better than reading a book or watching a movie. If anything, I wish the board was bigger and with more things on it - which means we're suddenly actually interested in Antarctica! 

PS: We house-ruled both Cursed and Blessed conditions to go away faster, as we don't particularly enjoy playing with either.

RATING: 6
Truly Arkham Horror Light taken to a world stage with significantly different methods to stop each AO. A refreshing change-of-pace to your standard Arkham game.

Would have been better if not for these oversights:

*Cards are more flimsy than standard FFG fare. One of my cards is creased already.
*Double-sided cards are often drawn by name, and it's unclear which side is supposed to be the "front." Handling these decks is tedious.
*1p games are not as one-sided as in Arkham Horror, but they still leave a lot to be desired and are disproportionately hard.
*Lack variety in encounter decks
*(SPOILER) defeated investigator encounters differ only in flavor text and the stat you have to test, and not in outcome
*Resetting a solved Mystery is not a substitute for difficulty and is NOT A FUN MECHANIC, PERIOD.

This game desperately needs expansions, but some of the above points simply can't be fixed this way.

RATING: 7
An epic game that immerses you quite well into a world falling apart from Otherworld Gates. While luck does play too much of a factor, this game is extremely thematic with tons of different ailments that can inflict you, while doing so in a way without being convoluted. Also, I love that going to different locations will cause different encounter cards to have different effects. Besides the luck and a few extremely evil cards the only problem I have with this game is that I kind of wish that that the Encounter and player phases were not separate, as since you only have 2 actions on your turn, each player turn doesn't have the feel of you doing something hugely significant (in fact most of the time the important stuff you will be doing is during the Encounter).

EDIT: I've lowered my rating because this game is just too long for how little there is in terms of meaningful choices.

RATING: 7
Essentially, this is a streamlined Arkham Horror that swaps the Arkham setting for a global adventure. I like it. While I've always enjoyed Arkham Horror, the clunkiness of the game with all the things to remember makes it a bit of a headache to play, and I often end up feeling I didn't really win because I forgot some important rule once or twice. Eldritch Horror fixes these problems. While these fixes might have come at the cost of a bit less variation in the gameplay, it avoids this by adding decks of missions and events that are specific to each GOO, which adds a lot more flavour to the game.

This game will undoubtedly be treated to a plethora of expansions, which the compulsive buyer in me will want, but I'm pleased to say that it looks as though there's room for them without making the game the incomprehensible mess that Arkham Horror with expansions is.

Components are what you'd expect from FFG. No plastic minis (I'm sure they'll sell some down the road though), but lots of cardboard. I did have some tokens split in two, which is annoying, but I think FFG has long since decided that cheap Chinese production and a willingness to readily replace parts is cheaper than producing top quality components in the first place.

RATING: 8
An expansive, rambling adventure that is an absolute blast to play.  Every game is different and every encounter is exciting.  The mythos dice will confound you, yet you'll always be ready for the next encounter.  The encounter cards (all 7 different types!) are location and situation dependent, offering a small sense of control amongst the chaos.

The game features perhaps the best starting cast of any adventure game, with 12 unique characters to chose from in the base game alone.  They match wits with the Ancient Ones, who wait patiently for their chance to awaken and consume the world. Besides the Ancient Ones, investigators will face a seemingly endless horde of otherworldly monsters emanating from gates that spawn around the world.  Players must race around the world fighting monsters, solving clues and closing gates in an effort to solve the mystery and halt the arrival of the Ancient One.

This game is an absolute must for fans of adventure games and/or H.P. Lovecraft's works.  Always a great time, always willing to play!

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Solo: Despite the fussy setup, needlessly large board, and some fiddly bits during play, this is a fun game. A lot of fun, actually. I finished my first game and immediately wanted to play again.

In my first play, my poor investigator never seemed to have a chance -- there's a lot to do, on a big map, with just one investigator. Playing with two (one of many options presented in the rules; I love that) would help, but would increase the fiddliness factor. Solo games need to be hard to be fun, so hard is good.

It tells a fun story, feels a lot like playing Call of Cthulhu in some ways, and takes less time than solo Arkham Horror (90 minutes for this play). I like the variety of character abilities a lot, and I love the variety in the encounters. It seems like the Ancient Ones, since there are only four and they have relatively small mystery decks, might get same-y after a few games, but perhaps not; this element does seem overly "expansion ready."

It's also a beautiful game. The graphic design is gorgeous and fantastic from a usability standpoint, and the game just plain looks great. It has a moody, drey feel that I really like.

For setup, I can't imagine playing without a Plano box; a 3650N hold everything but the full-size cards, standees, monsters, and gates. An opaque bag for monsters would be nice, but might not fit in the box alongside the Plano. Apart from that, it seems like it's just going to stay fiddly in some ways.﻿

The two rulebooks thing was very annoying for about half an hour the first time I played. Thereafter, as the game itself says, I rarely if ever used the Rulebook; the Reference Guide did its job well. I don't think I made any mistakes or missed any rules in my first play, which in my experience is a rarity for a game like this. Further plays should reduce the amount I need the rules at all, too.

I'm not sure I ever see myself playing Arkham solo again. Its length is a bug for me, not a feature, and this delivers what I wanted from Arkham in a tighter, shorter package.

---

Second play was a win, with better luck and smarter play, and it took 60 minutes vs. 90 for my first. Good all around.

---

Re-rated from an 8 to a 7. I haven't had the urge to play this in the past couple of years, and it just went into storage.

RATING: 9.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
£35 150(150) £14
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Eldritch Horror fixes many of the faults Arkham Horror has but also misses some of the smaller good things. While the duration of the game is usually a lot shorter (than in AH), it still can take a few hours easily.
The base game suffers from limited amount of certain cards, but this is easily (and cheaply) fixed with the Forsaken Lore expansion (which is pretty much a must have purchase for any Eldritch owners). Another problem is the usually hopeless situation with mysteries when the AO awakens, making it too tempting just to give up (however, this is easily fixed with a simple house rule).
The story element isn't as strong as in Arkham Horror and Mansions of Madness, but definitely serves the needs of most of the players.
Only notable problem I have with the game is how the mysteries are handed out in the game as some of them are too challenging if drawn too early in the game. Also the final mystery shouldn't require the investigators to complete the other mysteries as usually the time limit is too tight (it's easy to fix it with house rule though).

RATING: 8
I was lucky enough to playtest the base game and its first expansion so fair warning that it probably colours my view somewhat.

Of course the obvious question is 'how does it compare to Arkham Horror?' Clearly, the answer is OK, but I prefer Arkham to Eldritch. As can be seen from my ratings. Why is this the case I can't really express very well, but it may be that I find EH to be more of a board game than an adventure and it's the story-telling adventuring that I like so much in AH.

Sure, EH has more 'investigation' than AH, it even has more direct co-op interaction between characters than AH and the AO has more influence on the character of the game than AH. It plays perhaps a little shorter and is easier to teach with fewer 'rules niggles'. But, at the end of the day, AH is still my favourite game.

RATING: 7
 Arkham Horror was the second game I had ever purchased and it was a huge disappointment for me. I love the theme and I love the idea of Arkham Horror, but it ended up being too mechanically fiddly and slow for me with a lack of any interesting decisions or coherent story. I sold it after a few plays and every once in a while I drop its rating a little further as my dislike for the game grows over time. So I was hesitant to buy Eldritch Horror.

When I ordered the game, I also ordered the new expansion just to fill out the decks more. My girlfriend and I played a two player game against Azathoth without selecting a difficulty level. We managed to beat the game with the doom track at 1, but it felt like we didn't deserve it. We only closed one gate on the map and there were a few monster roaming around the board that we basically ignored. We managed to just be in the right places at the right times to deal with the first two mystery cards and the third one took some work, but wasn't too bad, while at the same time the doom track was being eaten up by some nasty mythos cards. It didn't feel like we did anything more than just draw the right cards at the right time. The story was more coherent than in Arkham Horror, but at the same time I don't remember much of what happened. 

I'm rating it at a tentative 7 right now, but it could go up or down depending on future plays. I do think it is a lot better than Arkham Horror. Just the reduction in mechanical complexity is greatly appreciated. I would probably play this rather than Tales of the Arabian Nights.

RATING: 7
Is it better than Arkham Horror? Yeah, definitely. Would it have existed without Arkham Horror? Not at all. Eldritch Horror succeeds both at streamlining the gameplay and giving it a wider, more global feel. It increases player agency without increasing their power. The different Great Old Ones all do different things to the world, and work their evil in distinct ways. The complaints? It only has four Old Ones straight out of the box, and it's still fiddly as hell. There's a ton of cards, a ton of abilities, and a ton of things you're going to forget to do when there's five monsters on the board, your friend is one point away from insane and you have to do a thing before the next full moon. Good fun. Complex fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]

[u]19-Aug-2016[/u]: This is a really well produced, well developed game from FFG in the Cthulhu world. It's a cooperative game where the player(s) control a number of investigators, solving various mysteries before the Ancient One is awoken...and the world ends.

This game is big. Time and space. There are tons of cards and tokens. Everything is excellent FFG quality. Can't complain at all in that department. I think the game's strength is its narrative cohesion. I've never played Arkham Horror, but EH really presents a nice story with its variety of card decks keeping everything in line. The gameplay is based on dice resolution, and that is something that doesn't always gel with me...especially in a game of this time commitment.

I do not have any investment in the Cthulhu theme and lore, and I believe this is what ultimately made me decide this game wasn't for me. Without that knowledge and inherent interest in the theme, I just couldn't stick with it for the long haul. I'm really glad I got this game and played it a few times so that I could get that Lovecraftian experience. And for those who enjoy that, you should really give this a go. It's a solid effort.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
I wanted to love Arkham Horror, but its misses were more frequent and more pronounced than its hits. 

Eldritch Horror addresses some of these issues, but after 4 plays I'm not so sure that the experience is improved.

Our first play was an easy win over Azathoth. Our next 3 plays were losses that weren't close. We got our asses handed to us by Cthulhu, then Yog-Sothoth, then Shub-Niggurath. None was competitive. They were experiences where we were out of the game, but played on for another 30-45 minutes without hope. The game does have a built-in timer, as you lose when the mythos deck runs out (mercifully).

I know that good play is possible, but it still feels like victory is determined less by good play and more by fortuitous card draws and die rolls. I think that, as someone who plays a lot of eurogames, I may just be expecting a game like this to deliver something that it isn't meant to deliver. It's an experience game that it more about the journey, and I don't think I'm wired for that kind of game. 

Note that all of my plays were 2-player games with the Forsaken Lore expansion mixed in.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If you are considering purchasing this game take one thing from my comments here- this game is NOT a faster version of Arkham Horror! It is merely a DIFFERENT version of Arkham Horror. I suspect many looking for a 'faster, simpler' version of Arkham will be disappointed!  You have been warned.

Regardless of what others might say EH IS Arkham Horror 2nd Edition in as much of a way that Descent 2nd Edition is to its predecessor.

This means it has the same overall feel as Arkham, but with several 'improvements' to its system including a stronger narrative link between encounters & the chosen Ancient One, flip-card effects & spells from Mansions of Madness, and a more streamlined turn sequence (NOT faster- just simpler!).

It is still just like Arkham Horror in many ways, including similar game length (2-4 hours), has piles of chits & lots of cards, too many fiddly moving parts, a ton of book keeping and plenty of doom & gloom beat-down from the Mythos.

I'd say although the game is slightly easier to play it is harder to WIN then Arkham Horror by a fair margin!

It DOES do several things differently, and some things better than Arkham Horror does. It also does some things worse too.

It might sound weird but EH feels like it has more luck then Arkham! Yeah they have made the investigators & turn sequence less complex, but now I can't adjust my skills, only improve them by spending precious time stalling on a certain space! I can't easily collect clues even though they are more important in EH because I now need to have an unpredictable clue encounter first! I can't predict what gates have in store- its different every time! This list can go on & on. Yes both games are random, but Arkham has some choices! Eldritch seems to have taken even those all away, claiming they are 'fiddly'.

One of the largest banes for me is the countless condition cards and Reckoning effects, which I'm honestly sick of after just 4 games. This game all but shovels them onto you, with barely a round going by without one or more Investigators getting conditions of some kind. Then when Reckoning comes around you have to spend at least 5 minutes going through all the cards, monsters & conditions, checking them, reading text, rolling dice, moving tokens. It bogs the Mythos phase down horribly and is often much longer then Arkham Horror's Mythos Phase!

The base game also has a serious lack of cards>variation to start with, the generic encounter cards being the worst offenders. They would be fine for variety if it wasn't for the fact that almost all major actions for investigators happen in city spaces so City encounters get read almost constantly compared to those for Sea and Wilderness spaces. This means you will get City encounters repeating very quickly in longer games/with more players games.

This can be fixed in shorter games with the expansion Forbidden Lore, but re-playability is still a pretty big glitch with larger groups.

Lastly the Coop element has been all but destroyed by moving the theme to a global stage. Players have a hard time reaching different locations of the map and/or each other almost constantly with very little actual interaction, leading to a real lack of cohesion. Eldritch Horror is like a really miserable fetishist version of a circle jerk- everyone's in a circle playing on their own and crying (the Mythos is a cruel misteress). If this comparison makes you feel weird and uncomfortable then now you know how I feel whenever I play this game, and that's not how it should be..

Will it replace Arkham? At first I thought it would, but as more plays get notched I'm starting to doubt its longevity.

EH has many great ideas, and I can see how they have tried to improve upon Arkham Horror. The problem is they have made new problems and found new ways to bog down the game with moving parts, making it just as fiddely & messy in a different way. It also feels more random if such a thing is possible! So much more depends on the luck of the draw with so many strategic points rubbed out for the sake of simplicity.

Now the cult of the new hype has passed I'm feeling a little disappointed with it!

I find myself lusting for the old & familiar corners of my dear Arkham. I'm sleeping with the new, younger model, but I miss you so much.

RATING: 7
NOT better than Arkham, but still good. A lot of the difficulty feels very cheap and inorganic though, whereas in Arkham it usually comes down to a roll of the dice. In the process of streamlining AH, they lost a lot of the soul and essence with this game. Rather than tell a short story of why something is happening, it tells you that something is happening, and you need to make a skill check because of it. Still probably a better starting point than AH is for modern gamers though. The double-sided cards are cool, and I like the way the store is handled more here. Even the way the Great Old Ones change up the game makes for a more structured experience, but the action point allowance system feels VERY restrictive here, and not in a good way. I don't mind Eldritch Horror, but I definitely feel like it plays you more than you play it. I feel like I have more control over what I want to do in Arkham, because if nothing else, it comes down to a face-off with the Great Old One.

RATING: 2
I love the world of Lovecraft and really wants to get into cooperative games. Yet, each game of Eldritch Horror leaves me unsatisfied. It's true that the components are good-locking and the texts intriguing. 

However, it all boils down to drawing one of the many cards and rolling a number of dice over and over again. The cooperation is limited to agreeing who does what and then wait for each other to perform individual actions (and the waiting time is looong - in sessions over several hours I've only had time to move around a bit and fight a monster or two before it's time to leave). Where is the tactics? Where is the strategy?

At least I get plenty of time between the turns to work on my own games.

RATING: 8
Had a couple of solo run-throughs of Eldritch Horror last night.
Got my arse thoroughly kicked in the first game, but pulled off a win in the second game with a single investigator.  Well, two investigators.  The first one went insane, but there’s a cool rule to replace them where the crippled/insane investigator becomes an encounter you can go and have with your new character.  If successful you get all their stuff and they help you out before bailing!
Basically it’s a re-tread of Arkham Horror with a world map, faster play, simplified mechanics, far fewer cards (because of all the AH expansions) and a more tailored theme for each GOO – they each have their own event decks.  Pretty impressed overall, but makes me want to break out AH to do a proper comparison.

RATING: 8
Definitely improves on the fiddliness of Arkham Horror.  Most Eurogamers I know despise Arkham Horror, and they will not like Eldritch Horror any better.  It is much easier to setup and break down than Arkham Horror, and has some simpler mechanisms.  At the end of the day, it mostly boils down to move here, move there, draw a card, resolve it and/or roll some dice.  That being said, I like it.  I enjoyed this solo and it was a fun, relaxing time.  You need to embrace the theme and the flavor text of all of the cards in order to truly immerse yourself, and enjoy, the experience.  

Edit:  I bumped my rating up to an 8 while I let me impressions simmer for a few days.  I think it has been a while since I really enjoyed a game this much.  The theme is so well integrated and having it span the globe gives it that epic feel that seemed missing from Arkham Horror.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
It seems to be an improvement over Arkham Horror. They are not the same, but they do the same function. EH is more streamlined and shorter: easier rules and the game flux is definitly faster.

At its core, it's move to X, draw card, roll whatever dice the card asks for, and check for sucess/fail. In that sense, it's a bit repetitive.

But the characters are very different and the personal skills is really important, it really makes a difference in how they are played.

You do the same things over and over, and you'll draw the same cards over and over (more cards would have been great; I guess expansions will solve this); ganmes will be different not because of the cards, but because of the order they appear, etc...

Up to now, I have played a 3 hours game that did not feet long (Yog Sothoth appeared on the world and we achieved an unlikely victory, so it would not have been longer with 5 players). In fact, it seemed quite dynamic and with open options, some discussions about strategy...

The components are fine, but miniatures for the characters would have been great. The theme is also well integrated into the rules.

I don't recommend it for more than 5 players. Too much idle time between turns.

About the difficulty, it's harder with uneven number of players. 

I expected this game to be something around a 7, but for the moment, I'm giving it a 8,5.

RATING: 8.5
I am happy to report this is light years better than AH '05, while being different enough from the '87 AH to keep both in my collection. It streamlines everything from the '05 version, expands the scope, adds a sense of adventure, shortens the play time, and gives you a goal to work toward rather than just a "put the fire out" motif. Setup can be a chore. Looking forward to playing more.

Winning with one investigator is a real challenge. You have to pay attention to everything at once, which is tough. I may look at extending the doom track for just one for those occasions when I want the story to play out a little more and not end so early.

Yeah so the only real downside here is the board is just too much to manage for one investigator. You have no hope of controlling the gates or monsters, so you just have to get lucky with the mystery cards and Mythos card draws to win.

First win: Cthulhu, play 6.

RATING: 7
心得:
很吃運氣的遊戲
基本上都在骰子檢定
而劇情則需要靠卡牌來有帶入感
但是光是念出來有時候也是怪怪的
算是偏RPG的遊戲
策略性還好
規則:
***設置***
*次元門洗勻蓋著疊起，線索蓋著洗勻，普通怪物全部丟進袋中，史詩怪放一旁
*選擇領袖然後每個玩家選一個調查員，拿起始物品(援助卡，法術卡)跟在起始位置，還有起始體力跟神智
*決定遠古邪神，別結算期準備效果，挑出對應邪神的調查遭遇，特殊遭遇跟神秘事件卡(看牌背)
*探險遭遇卡洗勻，法術跟狀態卡分別洗勻正面朝上，剩下牌暗牌背洗勻
*根據邪神卡構築神話牌庫，先分開綠黃藍，在根據邪神卡抽對應數量組成不同階段，組成後各自洗勻在I~III疊起
*放玩家人數參考卡，根據邪神放對應末日軌，預兆標誌放在綠色位置，援助卡開四張到圖版對應位置，根據參考卡開次元門出現線索，按照探險遭遇卡頂牌牌背放探索標誌在對應地點，最後抽一張神秘事件卡並結算
***遊戲進行***
遊戲每輪分成三個階段，直到解完三個神祕事件卡(沒復活)，邪神復活的話要解第四張來獲勝
1.行動階段 由領袖開始順時鐘輪流執行兩個不重複動作
>旅行 移動一格，可花費對應行動方式的旅行票來多動一格
>休息 恢復1體力1神智 只能回到最大值，有怪物不能休息
>交易 可跟同區域的調查員換物品(援助神器線索法術旅行票)
>準備旅行 在城市可獲得一張旅行票(有鐵路才能拿火車票，船票亦然)，上限兩張，超過選擇棄掉
>獲得援助卡 在城市可擲骰做影響檢定，可獲得檢定成功數目大於左上數值的援助卡，沒辦法拿可棄掉儲備區的一張並補一張，有怪物不能做 (可選擇拿債務狀態卡一張來獲得兩個成功檢定，最多身上只能有一張)
>使用各種卡片或自己能力 所有卡片每輪只能做一次除了地點行動以外
2.遭遇階段 結算地點的狀況
>戰鬥遭遇 有怪物先由玩家選擇對每隻怪物做一次戰鬥遭遇，如果結算玩沒怪物則可在選擇做一次遭遇
>地區遭遇 選擇對應地點或普通遭遇卡，每個城市有特定功能提升的遭遇
>標誌遭遇 (可選擇)有線索可抽調查遭遇卡，次元門抽異世界遭遇卡，探險標誌抽探險遭遇卡，傳聞標誌解決傳聞神話卡，被打倒的調查員可結算遭遇
3.神話階段 抽一張神話卡並結算
>移動預兆軌，圖版如果有對應的次元門，每一個就移動末日軌一格
>結算報應效果 順序 怪物>邪神>神話卡>調查員援助卡或狀態
>出現次元門 根據參考卡出現對應數量，開啟並放在門上位置，然後抽一個怪物在該區
>怪物湧現 對應當前預兆標誌的次元門出現參考卡上數量的怪物，沒對應的次元門就開一個次元門
>出現線索 根據參考卡出現對應數量，抽看背面放地點，特定地點的就直接放
>放置傳聞標誌 指定位置
>放置驚悚標誌 放在該神話卡上
>結算神話卡效果 持續效果的放在邪神卡旁
*怪物抽出時如果有催生標示，執行其效果
*三階段結束後領袖可指定新玩家成為領袖並開始新的一輪
*如果 末日軌道0邪神復活，邪神卡翻面，有寫失敗條件，所有調查員都消滅，卡牌效果導致失敗，神話卡抽光>>都會輸
*檢定為根據角色技能值跟修正值擲對應數量骰，只要有5/6就算一次成功檢定，卡花費線索重值一顆骰來做檢定(非檢定擲骰不行)
*技能最多提升到兩級
*法術跟狀態卡獲得時只能看正面
*被耽擱的調查員標誌放，下次行動階段立起來，在下次才能行動
*體力或神智為0時被打倒，末日軌前進一格，根據什麼為0放一個對應標誌在放倒的調查員身上，持有物放卡上(狀態卡跟升級不算)，變更領袖如果倒的是領袖>>神話階段結束可選新的進場，遭遇倒地的可結算遭遇來拿持有物，被吞噬的直接移除遊戲
*邪神復活後 被打倒就直接算被消滅，該玩家不能再進場
*戰鬥 先做心智檢定，扣成功數減掉怪物數值的心智數，在作力量檢定，一樣扣體力，成功檢定數造成對應傷害，超過堅韌值怪物倒地，傷害會留著
*被伏擊時抽一隻怪物來遭遇，怪物如果沒死也會消失，伏擊怪沒有催生效果
*隨機區域 抽一個線索來決定地點，該線索棄掉
*神秘事件一被解決，就抽張新的放在舊的上面，三個解決就贏了，如果邪神甦醒，還要在解決邪神卡後面的事件
*卡牌或線索用完就將棄掉的洗勻重來，神話牌庫不會，沒次元門可用就推末日軌一格(線索 次元門 怪物 都在場上就沒辦法在加)
*狀態卡不能重複
*法術狀態卡棄掉就丟回牌庫洗勻，怪物棄掉丟回袋中，當要棄掉對應配件卻不夠則棄掉所有該類配件
*雙面卡牌隨機抽取時抽底部，獲得隨機卡牌時抽對應牌庫底牌，重複則抽到不重複
***調整難度
有章魚爪的是困難神話卡，冰雪的是簡單神話卡
要增加難度可在一開始加一張傳聞神話卡，不增加線索
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I have fallen out of love with Ameri-trash games. They used to be so much fun when I didn't know any better. I loved the story, the abilities of the individual characters and the atmosphere the game creates. But, dear God, is this a terrible game to play with others who are apathetic about the lore and myth of Lovecraft's stories. You MUST play this game with fans of this genre who understand that this will take 5-6 hours to play, that turns will be interminable and that any attempt of strategy will be (usually) upended by a couple bad rolls of the die. The game is bloated with too much of everything: items, spells, clues and "tests" for everything. You never "get" anything; you have to get a good roll and that might be taken away from you later. Nothing in this game comes easy. The board is usually overrun and you always feel like you're in second gear the entire game. There is so much to do and the players usually feel so powerless against the bad stuff that I end up preemptively ending the game in favor for a better game. I would only show this game to people who love the material and have half their day to waste on a game that they'll probably lose. Sorry, Arkham/Eldritch/Elder Signs, you're a long climb for a not-so-fun slide.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It's a cleaner, more streamlined version of the Arkham Horror system, but they provide much the same game experience so there's certainly no need to have both. Everything you need to do is dependent on dice rolling. Boost your stats? Roll dice. Solve a mystery? Dice. Combat? Dice. Close a gate? Dice. If you have an aversion to dice, step to one side, this ain't the game for you. We're playing this one for the thematic ride, baby. There's lots of cards, all of which are dripping with story progression notes, and a few different big bosses to provide game-to-game variety. All of which will no doubt be boosted by the inevitable expansions. It's fun enough, especially when you can start hamming up your conditions to get the theme flowing.

RATING: 5
My primary complaints from Arkham Horror were:

1) Too random (flip a random card then roll dice to see if you pass/fail that card... over and over and over)
2) Too long for what it is (3+ hours is too long when you're just flipping cards and rolling d6's)

Eldritch Horror fixes neither. If it were 1-2 hours, I would enjoy it more as a fun, atmospheric horror-game; but 3+ hours is just too long when you're doing the same random stuff over and over.

RATING: 8
"Streamlined and improved Arkham Horror, but still longer than I would like."

What I like:
- Definitely better than AH
- Very few fiddly rules (especially compared to AH)
- OO-specific mysteries are awesome
- "Conditions" are really cool

What I don't like:
- Still too long (5p learning game 3+ hrs; 2p game 3 hrs)
- More encounter cards would be helpful for variety
- Sometimes letting a character die helps you win

My 5p learning game was a horrible slog, where we made almost no progress. My 2p game was MUCH better, and we actually barely won. It raised my rating from a 7 to an 8. 

There are TONS of things about this that are better than AH, and I can't think of a single think I like better in AH than this. However, I wish it would  have been trimmed by another 25-50%.

I disagree with those (like Rahdo) that there aren't many decisions to make. The game does have a lot of randomness, but it does also reward good decisions. 

I agree that the weight is somewhere above 3. It's hard to put a specific number on it.
