=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
The good:

I just love the theme of this game. 

The narrative is very good.

The items, allies, artefacts are all great. Characters are generally good too.

The bad:

It feels like FFG never had an original or elegant thought during the design process - just make a map and let the characters hop around it.

The rules are complicated by the sheer amount of cards and exceptions available.

The missions are poorly designed. Why all the different decks? I like that there are different decks per location, but then each god has 3 decks and ... urgh. Oh yeah, and then we have the mythos deck. Why for the love of god?

Combat is unintuitive and the damage and toughness ratings complicate it unnecessarily.

Pacing is weak. After 2 hours building skills, you find you still have no chance of completing the challenges and then you put it all back in the box. Like Arkham Horror, progress is so slow that I get bored after a couple of hours and just want my table top back.

Set up takes well over 30 minutes and is not a pleasurable experience.

Forsaken Lore improved things, because it added cards to existing decks without adding more rules. Yig is good too.

Mountains of Madness broke this game for me. More new decks! And how do I know when to use the Antarctic Deck vs Elder God deck and which mystery am I supposed to be working on? 

Strange Remnants adds some good new cards and I like the missions.

RATING: 8

Until you see a character covered in Markings of Isis and with a number of amulets and magic books in his pocket get angry at a sailor on a ship for "being unnaturally strong", beating him and slapping him around with strength check after strength check until they plead guilty to dealing with dark forces, you haven't had the EH experience. RATING: 10
Very solid, thematic game that tells a story every time. It might be that the outcome comes down to a single die roll but that die roll doesn't happen in a vacuum. It is a result of all actions and events through the game. There is a lot of risk management and situations where you might have to send a weak investigator to face a strong monster just for the possibility of wounding it and making the job of the monster killer slightly easier.That said, it depends a lot on personal preference. For some this will be a dice-fest with random and unfair encounters and mythos cards crippling investigators right and left.

The rules are pretty straightforward and have a consistent and precise wording throughout. The weight rating in this game comes mainly from the number of rules, components and resulting interactions and edge cases.

The base game has fairly few mysteries and encounters, making the Forsaken Lore an almost obligatory addition for repeated play.

Update: Playtime is approximately 1 hr/investigator plus setup/teardown of 30 minutes when playing by reading the cards aloud to each other. Can be played in shorter time by a focused group or solo.

RATING: 9.5
I absolutely love this game. I've played about 8 times now (solo each time) and cannot wait to play again. Having lost 5 of the games, I feel there is enough challenge to bring me back for more. As it stands, after playing, say, Azathoth a number of times I began to know what cards were likely to appear and thought to plan accordingly. I personally prefer to have the narrative unfold each time with complete surprise. But I'm nitpicking, frankly, because this is a wonderful experience and one of my favorite games of the past few years.

Co-op, streamlined version of Arkham Horror (but still a bit weighty). Lovecraft/horror theme is not our favorite. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game reminded me a lot of everything I liked about Talisman (Revised 4th Edition) without any of the things that annoyed me about it. In this game, you have a character you walk around the board, and when you end your turn in a space, you draw and resolve a card from the deck matching that space's type. The card usually requires you to make a check by rolling some dice. There are numerous ways of mitigating bad die rolls, but it can still happen. When you accomplish certain story goals, you activate more story cards, until either you win, or you flip the endgame card to see what your encounter is. Leveling in the game was quick and fun, which I liked a lot. It seemed like it wasn't too difficult to get powerful and interesting items. All in all I'm happy with this game. With 3 players I was anxious for each of my turns to come along, so I'm not sure how well this would play it's maximum number of players (8).

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A nicely thematic game with a lot of randomness.
I like the text on the cards that describes what is happening. It's just the right length to set the mood without bogging down the game. The game also throws a nice variety of challenges at the players to keep the gameplay varied.
However, unmitigated dice rolls are used constantly to determine success or failure, and it makes the game very swingy and potentially frustrating. There are some small things you can do to tilt the odds in your favor, but succeeding is based far too much on luck for my taste. For example, in my first game I played a martial artist who got a magical bonus to strength and found an excellent weapon early on. She was as good at fighting as a character could ever be, but she failed repeatedly when fighting enemies because of bad dice rolls. That's just not fun. Setting up and tearing down the game also take a very long time, with lots of fiddly little bits.
Overall, it's just not for me. It seems to be more about creating a funny story of failure than providing a game to play based on skill and decisions.

RATING: 8
I used to really enjoy Arkham Horror, and I still do like it. But over time as I've played more and more games, the issues I have with Arkham Horror slowly got to be more and more tiring. 

And now that I've played Eldritch Horror, I didn't even realize how many little things were as annoying there. But Eldritch Horror ultimately does so many things better: 

--Movement is better, including being able to move past monsters. No longer can you just get stuck due to an annoying monster movement

--Things are much more streamlined. No longer are there umpteen different exceptions and abilities on monsters. No more focus for moving around skills. Less tracking 17 different things all over the place. 

--Dealing with monsters and gates is much better here as well. No longer do you need to move to street locations and kill monsters but not get encounters, nor do you wind up having to travel through gates and not get to do other things. 

--Finally the health system is better where healing is easier and death no longer is as bad as you just get to start a new character rather than having to play as some crippled character. 


There were some other nice additions such as conditions and a few smarter things going on with the big baddies, but all around Eldritch Horror is an excellent adventure game. It tells a fun story that is full of interesting and dramatic moments which is always what these games were going for with their heavy ameritrash backgrounds. Sure there are still a lot of dice rolls and victory can often come down to a roll of the d6, but at the same time there are some meaningful decisions that determine if you even get to that point or lose far sooner. Along the way there should be plenty of yelling and screaming about the great success or failures, the surprising twists and turns, and the crazy events that occur. In this respect Eldritch Horror and Arkham Horror are not much different, but in my mind I'll almost always rather play Eldritch as it gives me those same moments with about half of the rules and complexity and less frustrating times where it feels I can do nothing.

RATING: 8
After first game: better than AH, streamlined, with a lot less book keeping and a lot more actual play. Loving it!
After another sixteen: great game, and that comes from someone who isn't a fan of ameritrash (or "thematic games" in the politically correct language). So what's so good in it? Well, it's relatively simple, a bit scripted, but still gives you a feeling of being involved in the story. It's certainly not easy and often very tense, and if you win, it's never by a huge margin. Quite on the contrary, we had many games end with a win in the last turn (witch cheers and laughter and an almost authentic feeling of relief after saving the world).

So, long story short: if you enjoyed the atmosphere of "Arkham Horror", but didn't like it's fiddlyness, get "Eldritch" - it offers the same feeling with smoother gameplay and huge replayability (especially with the first small expansion). I don't play it VERY often, but when I do, I always have fun with it - and with all likelihood that won't change anytime soon. So two thumbs up!

RATING: 7
A wonderful reimplementation of Arkham Horror that addresses the issue of randomness in the story lines from the original.  The amount of content in the box is just shy of staggering, but still looks to maintain a tight theme with each Great Old One having their own decks.

However, randomness still occurs in a large number of instances in this game.  The number of times I have attempted a task with a reasonable chance of success (~66-75%) only to come out with nothing should be studied, as I'm pretty sure it would redefine our conceptualization of statistics, or at least it could be the basis of a new branch.  Now, I'm being hyperbolic.  I'm pretty sure if you did a statistical study of my rolls it would conform to the expected results.  But it doesn't feel that way.  And I'm pretty sure it's because this game gives rather meager rewards for success, while crippling you for failure.  This vast difference in outcomes is fitting for the theme of the game, but it does leave a bitter enough taste that this game doesn't hit the table very often.

Unfortunately, due to the expansion glut of the original, I do not think I will be buying any expansions unless they dramatically improve the game.

RATING: 4
I've played this four times. 

The real value of this game is 1) its sandboxy feel 2) the genuine thematic tension 3) occasional (but much too rare) fun, risky decisions.

The problem is that you will probably be bored or frustrated for most of the game.

One big issue is its huge randomness. Unlucky combinations of cards/dice happen much too often and can virtually remove a player from the game.

The game isn't puzzly or structured enough to make a lot of its cooperative aspect. The vast majority of individual player decisions/actions tend to be routine, so the game can feel like it's playing itself.

Set-up time is painful and play involves a lot of housekeeping and card checking.

The game is evocative if you use your imagination and does succeed in making you fret about what will happen next. You'd be a frosty person not to laugh a few times when playing it.

If you want a more lively and competitive Lovecraft/Cthulhu experience try the excellent 1st edition of A Study In Emerald instead (oddly its 2nd edition is apparently very different). If you want a better co-op, there are plenty around.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Very thematic coop game with Lovecraft theme. The gameplay is pretty much restricted to rolling a different number of dice depending on which skill is tested. The decisions are limited to which region you should move, when to rest or which problem (mystery, gate, monster) to solve (I do not understand why the majority of gamers sees this as a medium or even heavier game; probably it is the game length).

While the game really does not offer much regarding gameplay, it really shines from a thematic and story-telling perspective. The components and the art is very good. You have different typical characters with different abilities and you fight different Lovecraftian monsters. 

Eldritch Horror is certainly a good game and I probably rate it a bit too low. I just put more emphasis on gameplay than theme and for my taste there were too few ways to mitigate your luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Arkham Horror Light, best with upto 4 players. This player restriction is in part due to the rumour cards which increase in difficulty based upon the number of players and is one of the weaker aspects of the game.

Having got a few plays under my belt, I can say that I prefer this to Arkham Horror (AH).  My reasoning is that each investigator is potentially equally effective.  What I mean by this is that in AH, players typically become type cast, e.g. magic casters attack magic monsters and melee characters attack melee monsters, in order to use the teams resources efficiently.
Within Eldritch, your character starts with stats which mean they are less likely to succeed various skill checks but through items and skill enhancements this evens itself out more.
Therefore each person's character is of equal value to the success of the mission and, teaming up is possible and at times a requirement.
The turns are also streamlined , although I fear that further expansions from FF may change that.
The choosen one who must be defeated also feels unique each time and their effects on the players and the world add flavour to each game.

Finally, the coolest thing is the reckoning.  Players are no longer restricted by money by way of improving their character's belongings.  They can choose to take a debt card for example.  Life goes on for said player, until the reckoning.  At this point, all cards in play with the reckoning icon are resolved.
You thought making a deal with those unsavoury characters in order to get that shot gun was a good move.  Well, its pay-back time.
If this could be expanded somehow to include skill upgrades, this would make a future expansion even more fun.  In Cthulhu style, where every ability comes with a cost, the path you choose is defined by your actions.  This can lead to some damned if you do and damned later if you don't choices.
Making each reckoning unique is  pure genius.  Dark Pact cards being some of the toughest to endure.  For example, with the option of being effectively devoured or given a dark pact card, our hero takes the dark pact.  Only two turns later failing the reckoning roll and having to choose another player to be devoured.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This seemed, on my one play so far, to be better than the average thematic dice-chucker. (It also seemed *insanely* hard - we died having completed none of the 3 tasks required, and with the doom tracker having hit 0 with only a few turns left of the 15 available.) I liked the way that things seemed to be set up for every game to be completely different (especially for each monster to have their own unique thing going on) - even down to cards with the same front but different backs, so you never know exactly what is going to happen when you flip them over.

Having said this, I still can't see myself wanting to play this very often. Like most of these so-called "thematic" games, it has a good story-telling feel but without feeling like there was a lot of deep strategy to think about. But I'd still be willing, and even happy, to play this at the moment. (I've still not played since that first game in January 2014 though...)

RATING: 5.4

The game play just feels tedious.

The game does a great job involving the theme into the game with art and interesting flavor text. But I'd rather play Elder Sign, which approaches the thematic quality of Eldritch Horror but does so with streamlined game play.

If I am looking to play an epic game crossing a world map and rolling dice to decide events, I'd rather play Fortune and Glory, which has a much faster pace and more excitement. The one thing that Eldritch Horror does better than Fortune and Glory is that it does a good job being thematically consistent (events are determined more by where you are on the board).

RATING: 9
I rate games using what their intended design was as a yard stick. This game is meant to be a thematic, dice heavy game that tells a great story. Luck and randomness are in this game, but can be minimized by playing smart and to your investigators' strengths. All this allows for a game with endless variety and twists and turns that keep you on your feet because anything can happen. It's fun reading the lore texts on the cards as you resolve rolls, whether with good or bad results, it really tells an amazing story thats different each game. Speaking of different, I highly recommend getting as many expansions as you can. They all add so much cool variety to every aspect of the game.

I kinda love-hate this game. I really enjoy globetrotting and building up my character, but the game kinda forces you to deal with other stuff (particularly gates) or lose. Character death happens easily but isn't super bad because you can more often than not recover the assets and doom (but not increased attributes) that you've lost. This game has also made me shout and throw components more than any other. I've churned through several (as in, roughly 7) of my characters die in one game. More than once I've wondered why I'm even playing this damn thing. But I keep coming back.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is the follow-up to Arkham Horror, instead of focusing on the town of Arkham, you're now globetrotting to fight off the Great Old Ones. From a thematic standpoint, it's a little weaker than AH, just because things don't totally make sense. Why do I go to Australia to gain strength? No gyms in London or San Francisco? The story is also more grand/less personal, and feels a little jumpy. I don't find that a major problem, but worth mentioning. 

While the theme suffers slightly, the mechanisms are improved a lot. Much of the rules minutiae of AH is gone in favour of a smoother and cleaner system. No more focus, no more money, monsters generally don't move, there are clear objectives in play, replacing the frantic 'run around town and plug holes' of AH, and if the Great Old One awakens you have a shot at literally defeating them. It's not a great shot, and it might be slightly anti-thematic to believe a few adventurers could dynamite Yog back to his(?) home, but from a gameplay aspect, it feels much more encouraging. In addition there's a really good new mechanism in the form of status card. There are status cards such as debt, injury, or dark pact, and you don't know what the drawback is until their triggered. So you may be traveling around with several personal demons and they might all confront and hinder you at once. Very, very cool. To top it off, the game plays in about 25% less time than AH, being that that's somewhere between 45 and 90 minutes, that's a considerable upside.

This sounds like a pretty glowing review, but honestly, playing just made me want to play AH. Eldritch Horror has a lot of upside, and probably the biggest is that in it's sleeker form, is just more likely to be played. I'm confidant I could teach the crucial rules to EH in under 10 mins, and I'd be happy to play it, but it doesn't offer the same level of immersion of Arkham. That's really it's only problem (you could argue EH has too few cards per deck), but that's the reason I play this sort of game, to experience the adventure of the characters, and Arkham does -that- (and maybe not much else) better.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
On first impression if feels like a cross between Arkham Horror and Tales of the Arabian Nights. Overall feeling is one more of a thematic ride than a co-op to be solved. 

Gates matter less or at least closing them doesn't [i]feel[/i] that urgent. Monsters can be avoided. The doom track is not as ominous as in AH. But most of all there's less of a need for cooperation and common strategy in comparisonsto AH. And the map of the world enforces the feeling that these investigators are moving in parallel, each doing their own thing. Like in TotAN. 

Another thing closer to TotAN are chaotic events - as stats of characters never change and items are hard to get by (if your influence isn't high) most encounters are very random in outcomes - they'll hit you, or won't. And you'll get certain statuses you'll try to get rid off (kinda like TotAN). In comparison to AH there's less tension and the feeling of sliding towards inevitable doom, instead it's more of a rollercoaster with some ups and downs (but more coherent, nowhere as crazy as TotAN).

What does it all mean? Well I'm actually happy they decided to move more into a story driven territory and made EH even more of an adventure game than AH was. On the other hand I'm a bit wary as how replayable the game is story wise (and how much it will need expansion). AH in comparison is more player driven and has an emergent narrative. EH take you for a ride, yet it is easier to get into and it does fix many rough edges of AH. It is easier to get into. On the other hand it doesn't feel as exciting or special, it's more even. Certainly there are remnants of MoM - the cards with different effects written on the back, the more story driven, yet less emergent, approach. Feels like this one is made more for expansions than AH. Maybe I'm weird, but I miss the gaps (and sometimes huge crevices) in the game that I have to feel in. It's more like being taken for a ride.

RATING: 7
30+ plays later, I'm not sure I'm in love with it as I used to be, at least as a 1-2 player game.

I still like it a lot for what it is: a fun, pulpy globetrotting adventure around the world trying to put a stop to an Ancient One.  But you're just along for the ride.    

Games can be an uneventful walk in the park, a quick and unceremonious defeat, or a long and drawn out affair where you just want the game to end.  You're completely at the mercy of the dice and mythos deck for what kind of game you may have.  

If you're OK with just going along for the ride and experiencing the story being told, great!  But I've just found that the amount of setup and upkeep involved for the game doesn't really match the level of actual engagement I get from the game.  Once you see the events enough time, the theme starts to wear off and it becomes less fun.

Expansions have added more Ancient Ones, events, monsters, items, etc, but rather than invigorate the system, they've ultimately felt just more of the same and made the whole system bloated.  

Also, having played Arkham Horror, I'm not convinced this game is any shorter.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I think this game may have completely replaced Arkham Horror for me.  Now, if you like Arkham Horror this game really isn't necessary.  I like Arkham Horror quite a bit myself but bought this game because my wife finds Arkham Horror just a bit too convoluted and fiddly and wanted to try this instead.  But I like this just a wee bit better (my wife a lot more so) and it is more accessible so I think I'll keep this one on my shelf.

This game is like a streamlined Arkham Horror and the game is going to feel very similar for anyone who has played AH.  In this game players again choose investigators and travel around a map (this one showing locations across the world) closing gates, defeating monsters, and finding clues in service of preventing an Ancient One from Lovecraft lore from entering our dimension.

Each investigator has special powers, as do each of the monsters and Ancient Ones.  There is also a wealth of spells, items, allies, conditions, artifacts, and more that add variability to the game.  The key difference between this and AH though is how the game is actually played and won (or lost).  Instead of trying to seal gates or defeat the Ancient One in a final battle, this game has a bit more narrative and the investigators are completing assorted different tasks called "mysteries".

There is still the sealing of gates to prevent more monsters from entering the world but the actual meat of the game feels a lot more interesting because there are different tasks each time and actually feel like they add to and do service to the story.  If the doom track reaches zero instead of going head-to-head with the Ancient One there is a final mystery the investigators can try to complete before losing outright.

Along with that there is the thing that attracted my wife to this game over AH in the first place, it is a lot less fiddly and more streamlined.  There is still a lot to set up but everything is more easy to understand and the pieces move a lot less in this game.  Also the rules are streamlined and more clear than those in AH so it is much easier to teach to new players and get them involved.  I will say that one downside to this streamlining is there are sometimes less options on a player's turn and we found ourselves occasionally wasting one of our two available actions because there wasn't a good option available.

Also, like other games with stories or scenarios as part of the gameplay content can be an issue (such as Mansions of Madness which I quite enjoy but would never spend money on).  This game only has 4 Ancient Ones and repeat plays are going to feel similar as you attempt the same mysteries repeatedly.  However there is so much other content in the 12 different investigators and tons of different assets, spells, and conditions that I think each game would feel different enough until you felt the need to buy one of the inevitable expansions (you know it's coming, just look at the amount of stuff they released for AH!)

Like I said, I can't recommend this game for someone who has and enjoys AH because it's not needed and most are going to want to stick with that game.  But if you are really looking for that game in a more story-centric and accessible package, then this one is a winner.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Rated after 12 plays.  If you had asked me 6 plays ago, I might have given this a 10.  It's an excellent cooperative game with a good amount of flavor and theme beset by some relatively boring mechanics.  I enjoyed the game immensely in my first 4 plays, which were all losses, and liked it a lot more when we finally won our first two games.  When you finally play the game enough to understand how to win, it's a euphoric feeling.

However, here is where the trouble starts.  Once you've figured out how to win, much like base Arkham Horror, it is very hard to lose.  We have since won 6 more games, for a total of 8 straight wins.  Over that stretch, we found ourselves growing restless and bored.  The problem is that the base game has an exceedingly poor variety in the cards on offer.  There doesn't appear to be much balance here at all.  Decks that will get used significantly, such as the Locations decks, only have 8 cards, so by your second or third game you've literally seen everything there is to offer.  Conversely, decks like the Expedition encounters, of which you may go on 1 or 2 per game, have 21 cards.  The other world deck is 24 cards, despite the fact that closing gates is not as big of a priority in Eldritch Horror.  It just feels like FFG got extremely lazy here.  The first expansion is almost necessary just to keep the game from getting too stale.

Beyond the problems with the decks being either far too small or too big, the gameplay itself is not particularly great.  Unlike Arkham Horror, rolls are not nearly as tense this time around.  Whereas you might have to get multiple successes to succeed at checks in Arkham Horror, in Eldritch Horror just one success is enough to succeed at almost every check in the game.

It's just little things like the above that add up over the course of time that unfortunately make this game feel like less than the sum of its parts.  I have purchased Forsaken Lore and hope that it will improve my rating of the game.  I am not holding my breath, however.

EDIT:  While Forsaken Lore does actually beef up the decks that need to be beefed up, it also adds my most dreaded expansion element:  cards that do nothing for you in order to try and balance the game's difficulty.  I absolutely hate it when an expansion does something like this in order to make a game more difficult.  What exactly do I mean?  Well, in the base game, Research Encounter cards that have an Attribute-1 test will almost always result in you gaining a clue and one additional clue.  In Forsaken Lore?  Such cards may grant you just one clue or even NOTHING POSITIVE AT ALL.  It is so deflating to pass a relatively tough test on a research encounter only to find out you basically wasted your encounter because all you did was avoid bad stuff.

Rather than making the game more difficult in an organic manner, such as making tests more difficult through modifiers or altering AOs' doom track starts, FFG decided to do it the arbitrary way by just not rewarding you as much or at all for taking certain encounters.  I don't like this change one bit and it has caused me to like this game even less.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Good game. Very similar to Arkham Horror, but gameplay on a global instead of local scale.

Adds some very nice mechanics to 'basic' AH: 
- spells each have individual after-effect, which you only will know after you have cast it. Same goes for conditions (you don't know what will happen to you before effect triggers).
- "Pass the torch" principle. Dead/insane investigators become lootpiles of equipment, but you might become grieved by their fate/stories if you visit it.
- Expedition encounter which moves around the map if you have 'solved' the previous one. This is very thematic and fun (expedition hunting).

Also adds some mechanics that don't really add:
- each 'endboss' has a different deck of challenges. However, these seem not tied in to these bosses very thematically (we used the wrong set and didn't even notice).
- you are no longer forced to encounters, other than monsters. So sometimes you can choose between having a Clue encounter, a city encounter, a Continent encounter, a gate encounter or an expedition encounter. It technically gives you more freedom but actually it feels as if you are more constricted (after all, only one instead of everything).
- a global market (called 'Reserve' for some reason). While this adds to the actions you can do in a turn, it is not a fun way to boost yourself. Better would've been a market tied into each location (so samurai swords in Japan, guns in San Fransisco, tomes in Arkham Horror, etc).
- each investigator has abilities that really drive your play. They don't only add, but really define how you should play with them. While this might be thematic, I can see it will wear off quickly, needing an investigor expansion (more investigators) to keep it fresh. Typical FFG style.

And finally subtract some fine mechanics of AH and/or add some mechanics that do not work:
- Personal sliders for abilities. In AH you had to weigh each turn where your character would try to specialise on. This made for some tough and fun decision. Here you have a set of static abilities which you can upgrade, much like in Runewars or Fortune & Glory. This is easier, but it removes a fun part of the game.
- You don't get a personal gain if you defeat a monster/close a gate. In AH you collected it as a sort of 'dark currency'. This really added to the feeling, whereas this feels more as Pandemic 'disease cleaning': necessary but in this game (where it is all about adventure) not the most fun to do.
- Travel has been implemented kind of sucky. Whereas in AH this was part of your tough 'start of turn' decisions, here you get a set of two actions to spend on various things. Worker placement style. Much like in World of Warcraft, this does not match with the adventure genre well.

All in all, while being a fun game, I won't be playing this over Arkham Horror which I like much more. Though the rulebook of AH is horrible, and it has lots of small rules which you might miss (so this combined can make for a frustrating learning experience), it is a much better game for my taste. However, Eldritch Horror is certainly not a bad game either, so if you want a decent Cthulhu game, you like F&G and AH was too fiddly for you, this might be the game you are looking for.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Eldritch Horror - I have always been interested in the world of HP Lovecraft. I definitely did not want to play the excruciatingly long Arkham Horror from everything I had read. When this came along I was instantly interested. I have played 5 times now and never won yet. It's really difficult.

Here is a mini breakdown:

Length - Long. Each game has been 2-2.5 hours. Luckily turns are quick so you keep engaged. So far Jessica's 'Bored-o-meter' has not gone into the red. That may be because she is on summer break. Last night I played with 3 and it took 2 hours just to try and solve the first mystery(you need 3) and then we lost. It has many timers: doom track, mythos cards running out, investigators being devoured and rumor cards coming to fruition. So it does end eventually. I could not see playing this with a large group. It would take hours. If everyone is swift and reads their cards quickly it moves along. April is AP prone and chatty so she slowed the game down last night. It's fine we lost anyway.

Difficulty - Hard. The basic mechanics are simple. All investigators take 2 actions, have one encounter (read a card) and then the monster goes. What makes it hard is that the game beats you over and over the head. Because the theming is good it doesn't make me mad that at that last moment I was about to solve a mystery my clue tokens were taken away by a draw of the card. You have to take it in stride that this shit happens in this game. Just when you think you have it, you don't. Usually at that point all hell breaks loose and the game ends rather quickly. That's also what keeps me playing.

Game play - Our first 2 games we just got beat up and never really got close to solving anything. Then I realized there was actual teamwork needed and efficiency. We got better, closer, we solved 2 mysteries of the 3...and then victory was snatched away by a horrible event. Grr. All hell broke loose and we lost. 

This is what happens. I do believe there is a level of play we have not figured out yet. Now that we know that certain crap can happen we are preparing for it better. We are picking better investigators for the job. You can partially mitigate the luck. I don't think you can prepare for everything (Especially with dice probability determining most everything). Also, each Great Old One is unique and makes the game play differently. That means new tactics will be needed. Very interesting. I'm hoping it makes for a different flavor each bad guy. 

I also discovered that you can take out "hard" cards and have an easier time. I'm going to try that just to see if we can actually win the game.

Final Thoughts - I like it. It's not as freewheeling fun as Defenders of the Realm. I really enjoy playing that one (chessiness and all). Eldritch is like a Pandemic on PCP. There is a lot going on. You can lose focus very easily. There are fires to be put out everywhere but you can somewhat ignore them for a bit. It's not cerebral. It's a discussion game. Planning is necessary but you can always count on being set back or losing that plan. You can't play it with the hope that your plan will succeed. There is a 50% chance it won't. 

You roll a lot of dice and read a lot of cards. Want to do something? Read card,roll dice. Want to get stuff? Read card, roll dice. Want to level up? Read card, roll dice. The theme holds it together but you must read the cards out loud or you will just be going through the motions. 

[b]It's a good co-op that needs multiple plays to enter it's world but a little bit messy and it's going to kick your butt. You have to enjoy the Fantasy Flight style of dice rolling and modifier cards. You have to accept and even enjoy moments of crushing thematic defeat. What compels you to play is the theme and "dammit I want to win this".[/b]

Expansions: Forsaken Lore

Fun factor : :star::star::star::star::nostar:

Fulfillment Factor : :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:

RATING: N/A
After two plays my rating is still pending, but I bet it will be as high as Arkham Horror.

Pros:
-The story element is a little more cohesive than in AH. This thanks to the clue and GOO specific cards.

- The characters are more personalized out of the box.

- Different backs to many boons, items etc brings more randomness and fluff.

Cons:
-The setting does not really do it for me. Sure, Antarctica, Arkham, London and Istanbul are cool places to visit, 
but regardless what the rpg fanatics tell you, Lovecraftian horror was born and bred in New England and there's
where I feel it should take place.

- You really don't do anything that much different from an experience in AH.

There is no way that I would delve into another monstrous expansion line as the two base games are as similar as they are now.
I do enjoy EH and love the experiences equally but as none of the pros listed above can not be remedied in AH in some way, I
presently don't see any reason to buy it.
I eagerly await the expansions though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
„Eldritch Horror: Przedwieczna groza”, czyli w 80 szaleństw dookoła świata.
Plusy:
1.	Oprawa graficzna.
2.	Solidne komponenty.
3.	Mechanika.
4.	Proste zasady.
5.	Klimat.
6.	Regrywalność.
7.	Przestronna plansza.
8.	Fabuła.

Minusy:
1.	Mała ilość kart spotkań i brak kilku rzeczy w podstawowej wersji.

„Eldritch Horror: Przedwieczna groza” to dobrze znana gra, której fabuła osadzona jest w świecie stworzonym przez H. P. Lovecrata. W pudle znajdziemy dużą planszę, na której nie będziemy mieć problemu z ciasnotą, a ilość talii kart uszczupli nam jeszcze bardziej miejsce na stole. Z racji tego, iż jest wszystkiego sporo, czas przygotowania gry jest odpowiednio długi. Polecam zaopatrzyć się w insert albo w kufer na grę. Przyśpiesza to przygotowanie i porządkuje żetony. Instrukcja opisuje zasady bardzo dobrze, a wszelkie wątpliwości powinien rozwiać almanach. Jeśli ktoś miał styczność z „Horrorem w Arkham” i/lub „ Znakiem Starszych Bogów” szybko przyswoi zasady Eldritcha. Rozgrywka, mimo iż trochę trwa to się nie dłuży. Historia, którą poznajmy wciąga i tworzy spójną całość. Dwie akcje do wykonania w turze to pozornie niewiele, ale sprawdza się to bardzo dobrze. Po wykonaniu dwóch akcji przystępujemy do fazy spotkań, i to właśnie teraz tworzy się historia i przygoda. Walka z przeciwnikami, sytuacje, które spotykają nas w każdym zakątku globu to właśnie to, co pochłania nas bez reszty. Nigdy nie wiemy, co nas może spotkać, czy coś będzie próbować nas zabić, a może pojawi się ktoś, kto wyciągnie do nas pomocną dłoń? Wszystkie te niewiadome i losowość w rozpatrywaniu testów i innych zdarzeń powoduje, że ciągle czujemy napięcie, a emocje mogą przechodzić ze skrajności w skrajność. W podstawowej wersji gry ilość kart spotkań jest mała i zmusza to do dokupienia dodatku „Zaginiona wiedza”. Brak kilku rozwiązań, które są znane z AH i ZSB też są wprowadzane przez kolejne dodatki. Pomijając braki ilościowe w podstawce to „Eldritch Horror” jest świetnym tytułem. Polecam każdemu, kto lubi twórczość Lovecraft. Moja ocena podstawki to 8/10.

RATING: 9
Very good game, after playing other Cthulhu based games (I am thinking mainly Arkham Horror and Mansions of Madness) this is the best one for me by far. It feels complex yet simple in its mechanics, smooth rather than abstruse. It doesn't take 2+ hours to learn, it has given us many hours of fun, good replayability with different characters and ancient ones... although I have to admit we have played against the same monster only once or twice til we beat it then we go for the next... and since we have so many expansions, we don't feel like we are repeating the game (even if with the same Ancient One it can change drastically depending on your luck, the objects you draw, etc). On the downside, indeed the luck plays a huge factor in this game... you are rolling dice for everything and you can end up very frustrated with how that turns out for you... you can do either nothing, okay, or very very good depending on how your rolls go.

RATING: 9
There's no way to not compare Eldritch to Arkham Horror:
1) Eldritch's worldwide scale makes way more sense than every misfortune in the universe happening in one small backwater town.
2) Eldritch is faster to set up and play (though not by much)
3) Eldritch is streamlined, but [b]not[/b] dumbed down
4) Having to solve mysteries instead of closing/sealing gates makes fighting different GOOs be a really different experience
5) The mysteries and clue encounters being keyed to specific GOOs makes the story told by Eldritch far, far more cohesive than that told by Arkham.

RATING: 2
I like how each of the Ancient Ones have their own unique mysteries that the investigators need to solve. The double-sided cards are interesting too.

Overall though, I prefer Arkham Horror. While streamlining a game might make it more appealing to the average gamer, it takes away its depth as well.

I never mind complexity in a game, so long as I feel the complexity adds something valuable to the experience. The amount of time a game takes is also never an issue for me. If I'm going to spend four hours playing game(s), I'd have more fun playing my favorite four-hour game, than multiple shorter games.

There are two reasons why someone might like Arkham Horror. They'll either like the theme of running around, solving mysteries and closing gates, while Lovecraftian monsters awaken, or they'll like the immersive engine, that is the mechanics and flow of the game. I like both but I prefer the latter. Someone who likes only the former might see Arkham Horror's mechanics as getting in the way of their Lovecraftian fun, and see Eldritch Horror as being everything they like about Arkham Horror, without the things they disliked. For someone like me though, Eldritch Horror, while having the theme of my favorite game, has taken away basically everything that makes Arkham Horror my favorite game.

You also go through the cards very quickly, with just the Eldritch Horror base game. After a couple of games, they start to get old. Even during the first game I played, some of the debt cards got old as we drew them multiple times, though to be fair, it was a six-player game, and we were all taking on a lot of debt. With Arkham Horror, I've played it countless times, including 10+ times with just the base game, and I still haven't seen all of the base game encounter cards. Despite owning all of the Arkham Horror expansions, I will still happily go back and play vanilla Arkham Horror from time-to-time. Now that I have an expansion for Eldritch Horror, I don't want to ever go back, to the base game. It feels like it was designed to give you the bare minimum required to play, to remain affordable, and if you want to continue to enjoy it, you'd need to get the Forsaken Lore expansion.

I give Eldritch Horror a 2/10, or 6/10 with the Forsaken Lore expansion, which fixes the cards-getting-old problem. With a side-board expansion in addition to Forsaken Lore, it's around 7/10.

RATING: 9.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]General[/b]
Well, I must start by saying that [thing=146021][/thing] is quite different from [thing=15987][/thing] in its mechanics. [thing=146021][/thing] seems to be an easier game to learn and teach, because there isn't issues envolving money (the purchase of items and the acquision of allies is all made through tests of Influence, and you acquire one or more of the shown cards up to the amount of successes rolled), nor there is things regarding injuries or madness (there are fixed effects, unique to the characters, and these informations are on the back of the characters sheet), or even special effects of locations (but the locations in [thing=146021][/thing] have different types of encounters, depending on what it is: city, wild or sea). Also, there is no more an upkeep phase - the attributes of the investigators are fixed (though they can go up with training) and the cards that require tests (such as blessings, curses, and other conditions) occur when a Mythos card activates them.

[b]Action[/b]
The round start with the actions phase in which each investigators has two to make, and they can't be the same. The actions are: travel (go to an adjacent place); prepare (in the next round the investigator will be able to travel to a local two spots away, by acquiring a train ticket or a ship ticket); rest (recover 1 stamina and 1 sanity); acquiring resources (by rolling Influence); and trade (if two investigators are in the same place).

[b]Encounters[/b]
In this phase the investigators battle monsters if there is one or more of them in the same place he/she is. If the place is free of monsters, the investigator can have an encounter there. There are several types of encounters and the player chooses (if there is more than one option) which to take effect. The types are: neutral (can happen anywhere in the board); local (for the named places, like: Arkham, Rome, Tokyo, and others); clue (can only happen in a place where a clue is); expedition (can only happen where the expedition marker is); rumor (if there is a rumor in place, the investigators can have an encounter in the active location of the rumor); and gate (if, in the place, there is an open gate).

Notice that, in [thing=146021][/thing], the Other World encounters (the gate encounters), now happen in the same phase as the others, not after - it only matters, now, the turn order, not which type of encounter is chosen. By the way, the gate encounters are solved all in one go (so, it isn't necessary anymore to pass for, usually, two Other World encounters in order to seal a gate), and the requirement for 5 clue tokens is no longer in place (though, sometimes, you will have to spend clues, but in a different fashion, normally to fulfill demands of the Mystery cards), and no place in the board is sealed - so the gates can always keep coming.

[b]Mythos[/b]
Unlike [thing=15987][/thing], in [thing=146021][/thing] there is a limited number of Mythos cards: the deck is made accordingly with the numbers written in the Ancient One sheet. The Mythos deck is, therefore, one of the time markes of the game - in case it is depleted, the game ends in defeat for the investigators - and this solves one of the issues many have with [thing=15987][/thing]: that the play can drag on, with none of the sides with much to do (the Ancient One has a tough time awake due to many seals in the board, and the investigators cannot acquire clues to seal one more - but are armed enough to kill the monsters, so the Terror track doesn't rise).

The Mythos card in [thing=146021][/thing] also works differently: no more indication as to where a gate will open, nor which type of monsters will move, or have different key types (except Rumor; so Environments and Headlines are out). In [thing=146021][/thing] the informations that can be in a Mythos card are:
- advance of the Omen, which will indicate which type of portal will be activated and, thus, how much will advance the Doom track;
- resolve reckoning effects (the red symbol that some monsters and cards have);
- spawn gates (a number of gates shown on the card open; every gate shows where in the board it will go). If there is no more gates to open, the Doom track advances;
- spawn monsters (one for each gate that opened);
- spawn clues (the number of clues on the Mythos cards are placed in the board - each clue shows where it must be placed);
- if the Mythos is a Rumor card, is activates a place in the board, and markers are put on it (this will show the time the investigators have to solve the Rumor - if they didn't, the game ends in defeat for the investigators).

[b]Tests[/b]
As I mentioned, there isn't anymore, like there is in [thing=15987][/thing], an upkeep phase to adjust stats. All the tests, now, are based in fixed stats - you just roll the number of dice equal to the number (modified by cards and other things, [u]but always rolling a minimum of one die[/u]). It is possible to improve the stats due to encounters or other effects. But, even with this, in [thing=146021][/thing] you are more stuck with bad attributes, since in [thing=15987][/thing] you could improve, in the upkeep, the stats of the investigator in order to prepare for something.

[b]Combat[/b]
This isn't much unlike the one in [thing=15987][/thing]: you still make an Horror check followed by an combat test. The differences, however, are several: first, you don't use a limit of "hands" to hold weapons - you can only use one, no matter which it is (so you will never stack the bonuses given by several items), but the benefits given by carried items (like, re-roll one die) can be used together with the weapon used. Also, you only make [u]one[/u] combat test - the creature must be defeated in only one roll, or it will stay in the place it is, and you will need to face it again in the next round if you want to defeated it - but the damage given stay in the monster.

Also, the monsters give sanity and stamina damage in a different way: you have an amount of successes to reach, for Horror and Combat. If you don't reach the amount shown, the investigator will receive, as damage, the difference. So say the investigator is facing a monster with -1 Determination and 3 Sanity; -1 Strenght and 3 Stamina. You roll 2 success for the Horror check, so 1 bellow the number of Sanity given, therefore, the monster will do 1 sanity damage. Then, again 2 success for the Combat check. The monster will receive 2 points of damage, will give 1 point of stamina damage to the investigator, and won't be defeated. The combat ends.

If there are more than 1 monster in a location, the investigator, after beating one monster, can fight another, and so on, until the investigator fails to defeat a monster or killed all of them. Finally, the monsters aren't taken as trophies anymore (since there isn't location to spend them), so all return to the pool of monsters when beaten (this do not apply to the epic creatures, because to them apply special rules).

[b]Spells and Conditions[/b]
One of the coolest things in [thing=146021][/thing] are the spells and Conditions, because these two types of cards have hidden information on their backs, and these informations can only be consulted when the investigator tries to cast the spell or when an specific situation happens (in the case of the Conditions cards). So, when this happens, you turn over the card to see what will occur. It is important to say that this mechanic isn't new: it was done somewhat similar in [thing=83330][/thing] for the spells, but, regardless of being innovative or not, I most certainly liked to see it applied here, since it is such an easy way to produce some tension and suspense into the gameplay.

By the way, the Conditions cards basically replace all that, in [thing=15987][/thing], were different things, like: Madness, Wounds, Loans, Blessings, Curses, and others. All the Conditions cards have two informations described in its front: a case in which you will lose the Condition, and a check you will make when the Mythos activate the Reckoning symbols (the red symbol). Again, an elegant decision, turning several types of decks in only one, more embracing.

[b]Leadership[/b]
In [thing=146021][/thing] the turn order is only fixed in a clockwise fashion after is decided which player will the the "leader" of the round - this player will be the first in the round. This way the players have more control over the game situation and also it inforces debate between players regarding what each of them will do in the round. Therefore is easier to react to changes in the game status and to act in a more cohesive way. Again, this isn't something new (for instance, the turn order in [thing=128445][/thing] is all decided by the players, it is not even clockwise after the first to go), but I do like more this way: it allows for some though situations (since the order isn't completely free to decide), thus putting some pressure in the players, while also allows to some degree of control.

[b]Winning and Losing[/b]
The investigators win when:
- all the 3 Mysteries are solved;
- fulfilling the conditions written in the back of the Ancient One sheet once it awakes.

The investigators lose when:
- the Ancient One awakes (accordingly with the rules in the back of the sheet);
- all the investigators are devoured (only a danger in the final battle);
- the effect of a card causes the end of the game (usually when a Rumor isn't solved in time);
- is necessarry to draw a Mythos card and there is none more to be drawn.

[b]Components e variety[/b]
As usual, the components from a Fantasy Flight game are great. Also, que quality of the art in every card and piece of cardboard is top notch - though, as it has become the norm, some of the art was taken from other games (why pay for the art and only use it once, right?).

The initial worry regarding the quality of the gameplay turned, once it was out, into another: does the game has enough replay value built on it? Yes, the game comes with a lot of cards and tokens, but, in some of my plays, all the clue encounters were used (but, since the encounters cards, like the ones in [thing=15987][/thing], are divided in three, don't take the "used all the encounter cards" as have read all of all of them). Encounters in some of the regions were also pretty close to end. But this isn't something new: since the base game of [thing=15987][/thing], as it came with only 8 encounters cards per region. Eight! I can't even count so high as the number of times that Carl Sanford asked me to join the Order (but surely it was enough for me to think that every citizen in Arkham was a member, some more than once). However, once the expansions were added, I now have decks of around 30 cards for each region. The initial worry was folly and I stopped shuffling the encounters after each encounter long ago. And there is no doubt in my mind that [thing=146021][/thing] will follow suit.

This is not only because there is already one expansion for the game. [thing=154842][/thing] was out pretty soon, probably to answer the more emphatic of the complaints. It comes with over 230 cards (to put in perspective, the base game comes with 303 cards). Therefore, as sure as the sun will come up and the Ancients Ones sleep, will come more expansions (and one more was already announced: [thing=164167][/thing]).

Now, in a more direct parallel: [thing=146021][/thing] comes with 12 investigators and 4 Ancient Ones. [thing=15987][/thing] comes with 16 investigators and 8 Ancient Ones. In the case of the investigators there is no argument: it is less, and it could have come with the same 16. But, regarding the Ancient Ones, not so much. Mainly because, in [thing=15987][/thing] you face the Ancient One always in the same way, suffering the same effects. In [thing=146021][/thing], the Mysteries to be solved can vary, not only in the order they might show up, but also for which will. So is indeed possible to face the same Ancient One in different ways. Besides this, each Ancient One has its own associated deck - the Clues encounters, to make the game more sound thematic. Also, the way to build the Mythos deck is different for each AO. With all this in mind, it is clear that is more harder to make an Ancient One in [thing=146021][/thing] (which explains the smaller number of them), while also allows for them to have a greater replay value, being more variable in themselves.

So, to sum it up, in the matter of replay value, I didn't feel "robbed". But one must have in mind the game is built to receive expansions (and, who knows, one day, an expansion for its expansions - I have seen it!). If you want a one-purchase-and-done type of game, well, avoid [thing=146021][/thing].

[b]Better? Worst?[/b]
Is [thing=146021][/thing] better or worst then [thing=15987][/thing]? At this point I can't say, not to one side and not to the other. They are way too different, having as major connections points the theme and the full cooperation. But since in size, duration and scope, they are closer than both are with [thing=100423][/thing], comparisons are inevitable. So here they go.

[thing=146021][/thing] seemed the easier game to learn and to teach, whithout as many cases of exceptions and little rules that are present in [thing=15987][/thing]. The duration of the plays also appeared to be shorter (though not short): from around 4 hours to around 3.

In [thing=146021][/thing] the monster don't block movement, so it is easier to go where you want, and even if a player takes the role of "monster killer" he will have a good deal of encounters, while, in [thing=15987][/thing], combat will more often than not means that your investigator will be on the street and won't have an encounter to make. The "passable" monsters, also help player to not "lose" a turn, staying in the same location for one or more turns, because one damned monster just won't move. Also, without Other World encounters, no more Lost in Time and Space (and I can't see this as a bad thing).

Besides, in [thing=146021][/thing] the goals of the game can vary accordingly to the Mysteries, so you not always will be after clues and sealing gates (though, both of this things still play a big role. So the players will do different things in different plays, more so than what happens in [thing=15987][/thing].

The downtime in [thing=146021][/thing] is similar to the one in [thing=15987][/thing]. And it feels as if the one in [thing=146021][/thing] is bigger since you do your whole encounter at once. The point in favor of [thing=146021][/thing] is that, since the investigator only has one combat check against any monster, fighting multiple monsters don't take nearly as long.

So, overall, pondering about the points above, [thing=146021][/thing] sounds like the better game, being without as many rough edges and also managing to have more theme in the flow of the game (but I much prefer the Allies from [thing=15987][/thing]). And this may well be truth. However, what seem like negative points for [thing=15987][/thing], don't bother me in the least - maybe because I got used to them, but most likely because I still have loads of fun playing it. So I can't properly be objective about this matter.

Anyway, [thing=146021][/thing] comes from a solid base, and the team of designers from Fantasy Flight knew how to work on the complaints and opinions received during the 8 years of [thing=15987][/thing] in order to create something mostly new, that has several merits. It already made a big splash among players (though, I don't think it will have the same level of success that [thing=15987][/thing] had and still have, but will surely be enough to sustain a steady line, with new material coming in a regular basis - and not the full abandonment that hit some of others great games from FF, ahem, [thing=31563][/thing], ahem, [thing=42776][/thing]).

So, I recommend [thing=146021][/thing], and this goes from a huge [thing=15987][/thing] fan. And yes, I do believe that there is room for both in the same collection. Though, if you are just now entering the this whole Mythos co-op thing, I guess [thing=146021][/thing] will be a better choice, since it is fairly new and will get way more attention in the years to come.

RATING: 9
The best Mythos game to date.

Even with all the customisation I've done over the years on AH (and all the money I've spent), I can't see myself ever playing it again unless as a nostalgia kick.

Even in a single play you'll probably see encounters repeated.  Forbidden Lore almost doubles the decks and adds a heap of replayability.

RATING: 9
Probably my favorite game. Fun co-op that isn't TOO complicated and fiddly unlike Arkham Horror, with nice materials and a lovecraftian theme, and it's challenging enough to require team effort in order to win. Wish there was a change of difficulty between an odd and even number of players though. 

The game length is perfect for 4 players, 6-8 players can take hours and hours. However, it also makes a very fun solo game, and is quite challenging if you just use one investigator. For any new owner who reads this review, I firmly suggest not using the "All for nothing" mythos card for it will lead to a bunch of frustrated players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like the idea of a co-operative game! Not only is it fun not to play against each other (well, that's actually also fun, but...), but it's also awesome to be able to play this game solo!

The idea of having an Ancient One and resolving mysteries to win the game makes the game so interesting, keeping the players constantly on their toes! The horror theme really gets you into the game, and allows you to be immersed in the world, role playing the characters! :P

The only thing that, I think, could be worked on, is probably the need to actually move towards and work on expeditions. Having only the base game, currently, I find no need to go for the expedition unless there is nothing better to do. This is especially true when the token is in some remote place (*ahem*antartica*ahem*). 

Overall, though, one of the best 'RPG'-like games I've played. :) Also, with the amount of things added from the array of expansions - one of the games with super engaging replayability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
We had chance to play a Lovecraft-lite game of 'Eldritch Horror' last night, which is essentially very similar to 'Arkham Horror' with a few less mechanics.

It sets the investigators against the same range of Outer Gods - we faced Azazoth, Ruler of Them All  - and we had to traverse the world rather than wondering the streets of Arkham alone.

I prefer this setting theme than Arkham alone. As with many Fantasy Flight Games, there are lots of cards for allies, artifacts, spells and weapons, with pre-set encounter cards.

This gives the game a lot of re-play value as no two games will ever be alike. The key is to choose a good range of investigators so we had a martial artist, the musician, a psychic, the soldier and one other.

We managed to prevent too many gates opening, and were very lucky on completing the three part investigation, before Azazoth awoke and wreaked havoc from beyond the stars.

There was only one casualty - my psychic, who lost her last remaining health point when the stars turned dark and she fell in Istanbul having healed her peers. A sad day. Boo hoo.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Cleaner than Arkham, and just blatantly a better game [i]qua[/i] game. Thematically, EH is much more coherent. AH's best plan of action is to avoid the story elements and abuse the location effects; the more I play to win, the less I like it. EH's clue and mystery mechanisms, and focus on encounters for resolution, make more sense for an adventure game, and don't require you to play in an un-fun, un-thematic way.

The biggest improvement is the game arc. Arkham's was predicated on a fundamentally wrong-headed core design choice with gates, doom, and victory conditions. In EH, you have an organic incentive to close gates (in AH, it was often counter-productive). And no more of the "coast to the finish" of AH (which might take a long time). Also--and this is big--less of the "buff up and fight", which I rarely did because it isn't fun in process or resolution.

Monsters in both AH and EH are tricky. AH had a lot of dumb elements (esp. the Outskirts, an obvious kludge), and the idea of all these things shambling around a small town was ludicrous. EH cleans a lot of that up, and the ability to avoid monsters and still get something done is very welcome (yet you still need to take care of them somehow). Again, player agency over RPG-esque effects; the overarching difference.

The GOO-specific focus in EH makes for [i]much[/i] more coherent stories. In AH, the GOO was often irrelevant (essentially one or two game effects, akin to EH's "ongoing" cards).

AH still offers greater character options, both in terms of setup (skills and variable starting possessions are missed). EH is tighter and more careful about these things, but I do miss some of AH's zaniness and character variability. The characters are more of a resource to manage in EH; having them do their job and then die, and then the new one goes and picks up their stuff, is common. Again, less RPG, more game.

Game length is only slightly improved. Still too many idiotic "lose a turn" effects in both; EH is better on this score, but no idea why those were retained at all.

I also really like the ease of expansion integration. There is no analogue to the "all-in" option with AH, but it's so much cleaner for it. The current approach is sustainable for a long time. And of course this sort of game is fine with house rules.

So, for me, a better experience overall, though I'll still keep and play Arkham as a "special event." But--shockingly--using AH as a starting point and learning some lessons from it, EH is just a blatantly better game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I love the theme, but there's just one fundamental issue with this game: there's too much card reading and not enough action. I don't mind a little bit of fluff, but unfortunately the action phase in this game is not interesting at all, players are very limited in what they can do. There's not enough real decision making, everything comes down to "move to another location, wait for the encounter phase, draw a card, read a card, roll some dice and hope for fives or sixes". I wish the game was more focused on the action phase. Unfortunately, it's very heavy on the encounter phase and by that it feels very "automatic". I'm sure some players will find this interesting, but I prefer cooperative games that offer more interesting choices (like Robinson Crusoe). 

Also, I was disappointed that the narrative in Eldritch Horror consists of just a bunch of unrelated events - I hoped for a more cohesive storyline (Mice & Mystics did it just right in terms of storytelling).

Still, I'm glad I tried it, at least I know that pure ameritrash cooperative games aren't really for me. No theme-heavy board game will ever be as immersive as good old RPGs. Do yourself a favour and find a good game master and try one of the Cthulhu universe-based RPG systems, you won't regret it.

RATING: 7
30/11/14: Secret Santa Gift, 2013: No matter what they say, this is FFG's Arkham Horror version 2; but it is, I have to grudgingly admit, better.  Some of what makes Arkham Horror great is lost: the focus on your character, the tactical choices offered by the sliders and the equipment assigned to your hands, and the bonkers final battles with the GOO.  But so much more is gained: more options on your turn; streamlined play with a stronger narrative focus on the GOO; and a deck of mythos cards that ticks down to a definite end, preventing games from lasting 6+ hours. 02/07/15: But do I like it?  Often whilst playing, I find myself looking forward to the end.  I love the stories it tells--but as a game, it's very dependant on the people you play with.  If they're not in on the joke that is Lovecraft's writing, the game can be tedious.  Too random for real strategy, Eldritch Horror has to be enjoyed for the ride, or avoided.

RATING: 7
For years, Arkham Horror was a near-buy for me.  I never took the leap, though, because of the game's length, obsessive mechanics, and messy rulebook.  I was happy to play occasionally under someone else's tutelage, but I had no interest in buying my own copy, struggling with the rules, and teaching it to newcomers.

Eldritch Horror is a cleaner, simpler game - but without being simplistic.  Also, the conditions and other double-sided cards are cool. I'm grateful that I don't have pounds of Arkham clogging my shelves, as EH has plenty of theme and silliness to keep my interest.  And if I only play semi-regularly, I can stick with the small-box expansions to spice things up.

For those who already own Arkham Horror, I can't imagine that you'd want to own both this version and the original.  Eldritch Horror is more of the same, really, in a slightly different form.  I'm not even sure it's any shorter.  But if you're bored and sleeping on piles of cash, I say go for it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
A beautiful looking game with a curious narrative, you work with your friends to save the world from a mysterious evil.  As great as that sounds, this coop game relies so much on 1/3 success chance dice roll that any good plans will become foiled and the game session will quickly become unbeatable. There is so much downtime watching your friends failing their rolls, while at the same time you're unable to deal with the cruel events that already happened to your character.  The game is the definition of a bad luck simulator.  A good upgrade from Arkham, but this game is still more upsetting than enjoyable.

RATING: 7

We'll see how I feel about it with more plays and whatever comes with expansions.  I enjoy it quite a bit so far and seems to move quicker (per turn) than Arkham.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[b]Eldritch Horror[/b] is the spawn of [i]Arkham Horror[/i] and largely [i]Elder Sign[/i], with a light sprinkling of [i]Mansions of Madness[/i] dust over the top.

The game's strip-mined all the other Cthulhu games for ideas, and is better for it, I think. And some of the systems used here are simple and effective, but given clever theme-inspired bells and whistles. I particularly like the Omen dial, with constellations depicted. The stars truly are right!

Multiple story-driven encounters are fun, and the choice of encounters to do later in the game is pretty awesome - however, the contents of these desperately need a boost in the arm. As many others have said, there are too few encounter cards for the cities and general locations, which is easily solved by an expansion. I would have preferred more cards in the game-box from the off, maybe even double what is presented, and paid a greater sum for the game - I was going to buy the game regardless of cost anyhow.

I feel, from my brief exposure, that solo quests are rock hard - which is nice, but feels at odds with the fact that [i]Eldritch Horror's[/i] A.I doesn't discriminate between solo and 2-player games.

In EH's favour, it is one of the few games of recent memory that doesn't seem to have any thematic conflicts. All the rules fit the theme particularly well, I've found.

Gloriously, all the conditions have a 'kicker' on their reverse side. This I love. For instance, if your character takes on a debt to gain assets, sometime later 'somebody' is going to come knocking asking for the debt to be repaid. Could be a loan-shark, or it might turn out to be someone much, much shadier. All the flip-sides are different, which means you'll never know what the possible punitive effect is until it's too late.

Another plus: the twin rulebooks are a triumph of layout and thought.

RATING: N/A
The surprise hit of 2015. I picked this up around the same time as Cosmic Encounter, more or less on impulse, because 'we didn't have a Cthulhu game yet'. I thought we'd get a few plays out of it before we saw all the cards and it became stale. Not since assuming Super Smash Bros on the Nintendo 64 would be a one-day wonder have I been so glad to be wrong about a game. 

Eldritch has bumped our modded Wiz-War off the no. 1 spot for the past year. It's seen dozens of plays and gets better with every game. Played solo it's creepy. Played with a group it's hilariously shlocky. Only the game length stops it utterly dominating every boardgame day. I'm also grateful to Eldritch for rekindling my interest in HP Lovecraft's fiction, which I'd read as a teenager but never quite appreciated.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
EH is one of my most played games so obviously i enjoy it a lot even though i am not a Cthulu fan. It's an enjoyable, masochistic, experience. I would only play it with a maximum of 4 players. 

It's an adventure. The journey is much more important than the destination and you have to immerse yourself in the game's theme. This means reading the flavor text and some role playing. Otherwise it can get dull.

This is important because the more you play it the more you realise that the decision making is pretty light. Most of the time it's pretty clear what actions to take. A few times you might also be looking for things to do. The dice rolling will determine, for the most part, how well the investigators will do.

Having said that i do think that the prioritization is important and interesting i.e. deal with a Mystery, a Rumour or Gates/Monsters.  

A very enjoyable game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun, but long... 4-5 hours with 6 players the first time. Longer with a game that went the distance.

And yet despite the length, I do want to play it. It's a nice adventure/wackamole coop, with some character improvement and good flavour, and generates hilarity. Some of the events are gratuitously bad though, in an unfun way (unsolve a mystery? yuk), and the balance is hard to finesse, with blowouts either way being my experience so far rather than a finely tuned down-to-the-last-turn affair.

Some house rules or expansions* could probably tweak the latter (there are decent options in the rules for modifying difficulty as well), but I'm not sure how to get the time down, so I guess you just need to be in it for the long haul.

I'll probably suggest the "Focus" action from one of the expansions the next time I play, and maybe nerfing Delay effects (use 1 Move action to un-Delay (without moving)) because being delayed is a bit boring. Also, it really feels like killing a monster or closing a gate should "get" you something (yes I know you've delayed doom and that's the real reward). Maybe one of the aforementioned Focus tokens?
